==PAGE:1==
Master’s Thesis
Using data-driven state-of-the-art machine learning and
conformal prediction for track irregularities from
observed dynamics of in-service railway vehicles
Author
Andreas Plesner - s174505
Supervisors
Allan Peter Engsig-Karup
Hans True
Stefano Bruni
Kongens Lyngby 2022
==PAGE:2==
Technical University of Denmark
Department of Applied Mathematics and Computer Science
Richard Petersens Plads, building 324,
2800 Kongens Lyngby, Denmark
Phone +45 4525 3031
compute@compute.dtu.dk
www.compute.dtu.dk
==PAGE:3==
Summary
This work uses data-driven machine learning methods to predict track distur-
bances using the dynamics of in-service railway vehicles and to estimate the
uncertainties of the predictions. The prediction task is formulated as the in-
verse problem of railway vehicle modelling, i.e. predicting the rail irregularities
from the dynamics. Data-driven methods solve the problem using function ap-
proximation by calibrating machine learning models using measurement data
via optimization methods. The machine learning models make point-to-point
predictions, from dynamics to irregularities, or make time-series to time-series
predictions.
A vehicle model based on the Cooperrider’s bogie is presented and extended to
curved tracks, and the model is implemented in Julia, a programming language
used extensively for scientific computing. Choosing Julia allows the utilization
of various built-in stiff ODE solvers. The prediction problem is formally defined
as approximating an inverse function from vehicle dynamics to rail irregularities,
and benchmarks are introduced. The problem is solved if, amongst other factors,
the mean unsigned error is less than 0.1 mm, and a model is satisfactory if,
amongst other factors, the mean unsigned error is less than 0.33 mm.
The
former is based on industry requirements, while the latter would put the model
on par with state-of-the-art solutions.
The real-world data from an ETR500 passenger train are presented and exam-
ined for outliers, and the outliers are handled using selected standard methods.
Afterwards, the data are formatted for the machine learning tasks used in the
project.
Finally, the data are split into training, validation and testing sets
which are used respectively to fit model coefficients, tune hyperparameters, and
estimate the generalization error of the final model.
==PAGE:4==
ii
Basic machine learning concepts are introduced, classic methods making point-
to-point predictions are presented, their hyperparameters are tuned, and they
are fitted to the data; the methods include a linear model and random forests.
These models are not satisfactory, achieving, at best, a mean unsigned error of
0.83 mm on the validation data.
Convolutional neural networks are presented as an efficient method for time-
series to time-series prediction. The method is presented in detail, along with
regularization techniques, such as dropout and weight decay. Cross-validation,
line searches and grid sampling are used to tune the relevant hyperparameters.
An in-depth error analysis is then performed to analyze the data surrounding
significant errors. This analysis shows that some data modifications were sub-
optimal and may negatively influence the model’s performance. Nevertheless,
the final CNN model is satisfactory regarding the mean unsigned error; 0.32 mm
on the validation data and 0.31 mm on the test data. The model can process
more than 2,000 km of track data per hour and can therefore be used to get
real-time predictions of the track irregularities.
Lastly, conformal prediction is presented as a data-driven method to construct
prediction intervals for unseen data. One of the methods is capable of capturing
97.18 % of the unseen samples from the test set using intervals with an average
width of 2.33 mm and a maximum width of 5.25 mm. This method can process
more than 1,000,000 km of track per hour when given the model predictions. It
can, therefore, easily be applied to in-service railway vehicles for processing the
predictions in real-time.
In conclusion, the project investigated data-driven methods for solving the in-
verse problem of railway modelling.
The obtained results suggest that it is
feasible to produce accurate time-series to time-series predictions and estimate
uncertainty intervals for the predictions. It thus enables real-time monitoring
of rail irregularities using the dynamics of in-service vehicles.
==PAGE:5==
Resumé
Dette arbejde benytter datadrevne maskinlæringsmetoder til at forudsige spor-
afvigelser ved brug af køredynamikken for jernbanekøretøjer i drift og til at esti-
mere usikkerheden ved disse forudsigelser. Forudsigelsesproblemet bliver formu-
leret som et inverst problem af modellering af et jernbanekøretøjs køredynamik.
Det vil sige at køredynamikken bruges til at forudsige skinneuregelmæssigheder.
De datadrevne metoder bruger funktionsapproksimation til at løse problemet
ved at kalibrere maskinlæringsmetoder til data ved hjælp af optimeringsme-
toder. Maskinlæringsmetoderne laver enten punkt til punkt forudsigelser eller
tidsrække til tidsrække forudsigelser.
Først præsenteres en model for et jernbanekøretøj baseret på Cooperrider’s bogie
og denne model udvides til at håndtere kurvede spor. Modellen implementeres i
Julia, som er et programmeringssprog, der bruges meget til videnskabelig data-
behandling. Brugen af Julia muliggør anvendelsen af forskellige løsere til et stift
system af ordinære differentialligninger. Forudsigelsesproblemet bliver formelt
defineret som approksimation af en invers funktion fra køredynamik til skinn-
euregelmæssigheder, og benchmarks introduceres. Problemet er løst, hvis blandt
flere faktorer den gennemsnitlige usignerede fejl er mindre end 0,1 mm, og en
model er tilfredsstillende, hvis blandt flere faktorer den gennemsnitlige usigne-
rede fejl er mindre end 0,33 mm. Førstnævnte er baseret på industriens krav,
mens sidstnævnte ville sætte modellen på niveau med state-of-the-art løsninger.
Data fra et ETR500-passagertog præsenteres og undersøges for dataafviglser, og
afvigelserne håndteres ved hjælp af udvalgte standardmetoder. Bagefter forma-
teres dataene til maskinlæringsopgaverne anvendt i projektet. Til sidst opdeles
dataene i trænings-, validerings- og testsæt, som bruges til henholdsvis at tilpas-
se modelkoefficienter, tilpasse hyperparametre og estimere generaliseringsfejlen
==PAGE:6==
iv
for den endelige model.
Derefter introduceres grundlæggende begreber for maskinlæring, klassiske meto-
der, der laver punkt-til-punkt forudsigelser, præsenteres, deres hyperparametre
tilpasses, og de tilpasses til dataene; metoderne omfatter blandt andet lineære
modeller og random forests. Disse modeller er ikke tilfredsstillende, idet de i
bedste fald opnår en gennemsnitlig usigneret fejl på 0,83 mm på valideringsda-
taene.
Konvolutionelle neurale netværk præsenteres som en effektiv metode til tids-
række til tidsrække forudsigelse. Metoden præsenteres i detaljer sammen med
regulariseringsteknikker, såsom dropout og weight decay. Krydsvalidering, linje-
søgninger og grid-sampling bruges til at tilpasse de relevante hyperparametre.
Der udføres derefter en dybdegående fejlanalyse for at analysere dataene om-
kring væsentlige fejl. Denne analyse viser, at nogle dataændringer var subop-
timale og kan påvirke modellens ydeevne negativt. Ikke desto mindre er den
endelige CNN-model tilfredsstillende med hensyn til den gennemsnitlige usigne-
rede fejl; 0,32 mm på valideringsdataene og 0,31 mm på testdataene. Modellen
kan behandle mere end 2.000 km spordata i timen og kan derfor bruges til at
lave forudsigelser af sporafvigelserne i realtid.
Endelig præsenteres conformal prediction som en datadreven metode til at kon-
struere usikkerhedsintervaller for usete data’s forudsigelser. En af metoderne er
i stand til at fange 97,18 % af de usete datapunkter fra testsættet ved hjælp af
intervaller med en gennemsnitlig bredde på 2,33 mm og en maksimal bredde på
5,25 mm. Denne metode kan behandle mere end 1.000.000 km spor i timen, når
modelforudsigelserne gives. Den kan derfor nemt anvendes på jernbanekøretøjer
i drift til behandling af forudsigelserne i realtid.
Projektet har undersøgt datadrevne metoder til løsning af det inverse problem
for jernbanemodellering. De opnåede resultater tyder på, at det er muligt at
producere nøjagtige tidsrække til tidsrække forudsigelser og estimere usikker-
hedsintervaller for forudsigelserne. Det muliggør således overvågning i realtid af
sporafvigelser ved hjælp af dynamikken for køretøjer i drift.

Preface
This thesis was prepared at DTU Compute under the supervision of Hans True,
Allan Peter Engsig-Karup and Stefano Bruni to fulfil the requirements for ac-
quiring a Master of Science degree in Engineering at the Technical University
of Denmark (DTU). The thesis results from collaborations with Stefano Bruni
from Dipartimento di Meccanica at Politecnico di Milano. The work was done
entirely at DTU, and Stefano Bruni provided the necessary data. The project
commenced on February 28th 2022, and ended on August 28th 2022, when it
was submitted to the examiners.
Lyngby, 28-August-2022
Andreas Lindhardt Plesner

==PAGE:15==
Chapter 1
Introduction
Until the second world war, railways were amongst the most popular means of
transportation for long distances, transporting people and cargo. However, the
rise of cars, trucks and aeroplanes after the war diminished railways’ popularity.
Despite this, railways have remained a commonly chosen transportation option
for medium distances. Several factors influence consumers’ choices regarding
their mode of transportation, such as ease of use, availability, time, price, com-
fort, safety, and environmental impact. Increasing congestion on the roads and
increasing fuel prices offset the comfort of cars. Also, in recent years, the en-
vironmental impact of transportation has become a big focus area as countries
aim towards reducing their carbon footprints, giving an advantage to rail whilst
making cars and aeroplanes less attractive.
Development towards electrifying the railways, increasing the speeds and com-
fort, and similar projects are making railways a fast, comfortable, cost-effective
and environmental mode of transportation which can fuel a resurgence in the
popularity of railways.
However, safety is one of the most critical factors for any mode of transportation.
Railways can be the greenest option, but if they are not safe, people will not use
them. One of the significant factors influencing the safety of a railway vehicle is
the wear and tear of the components, but crucially also, the wear and tear of the
rails. As railway vehicles operate at high speeds, they exert high pressure on the
==PAGE:16==
2
Introduction
rails, which over time and use, can deform the rails, and if track maintenance is
neglected, it may lead to a derailment. Even if this worst-case scenario does not
occur, the deformations may still cause flange contact or even flange climbing,
which can be very uncomfortable for the passengers.
Repairing critically deformed rails is an expensive operation. However, if done
before the deformations become critical, they can be done overnight, causing
minimal obstruction to the traffic and timetables. It is, therefore, of high interest
to capture the deformations as they start forming and while they are easily
fixable.
The standard method used to monitor the condition of the rails is to use high-
accuracy measuring vehicles. However, these are expensive, few and slow, so
they can only infrequently monitor a track segment. A better option would
be to install inexpensive sensors on in-service vehicles, such as passenger cars,
freight cars, or locomotives, and then process the collected data to monitor the
track condition over time. This method would allow a day-by-day view of the
track conditions, making it possible to catch deformities early.
This work focuses on taking sensor data from a high-speed ETR500 passenger
train in Italy, provided by Stefano Bruni, and using state-of-art data-driven ma-
chine learning models to process the collected sensor data and predict the track
irregularities. Additionally, the model should provide uncertainty measures of
the predictions without making assumptions about the underlying data distri-
butions.
The predictions enables continuous monitoring of the track quality
using industry-standard tools and methods.
Firstly, a realistic vehicle model of a railway car consisting of Cooperrider’s
bogies developed at DTU Compute will be presented in Chapter 2, along with
numerical methods to address the vehicle dynamics problem and differences
between the presented vehicle model and the ETR500 passenger train. Further-
more, this chapter will formalize the problem as solving an inverse problem of
the dynamics problem, i.e. going from dynamics to irregularities. Chapter 3
presents the provided data, identifies issues found in the data, and explains the
necessary steps to address those issues. Chapter 4 introduces machine learning
and presents classic machine learning models and how these perform on the in-
verse problem. Chapter 5 presents convolutional neural networks, CNNs, and
motivates using these over classical methods. Then an overview of the results
using these on the inverse problem is given. Chapter 6 introduces conformal pre-
diction as a method to provide data-driven uncertainty measures and presents
results when used on the optimal machine learning model.
==PAGE:17==
3
Literature review
Work by [LCK+12] shows that the vehicle’s dynamics are linked to the track
condition, thus supporting the feasibility of using the dynamics to assess the
track quality. [Rav19] has shown the benefits to railway operators of proac-
tive maintenance, and the idea of using in-service railway vehicles to moni-
tor the track conditions continuously has been around for a couple of decades
[WRYS15, KYT02]. Multiple sources have tackled the problem in various ways.
However, the methods have primarily focused on using more classical mathemat-
ical analysis tools, such as Kalman filters [LCK+12, MnRUE21a, MnRUE21b],
system identification [KYT02], digital and analogue processing [NKO10], and
other signal processing methods [TH20, WLJ16, BBF21, CBKK17] with a fo-
cus on interpretable models [dRKQ+21]. These methods face some underlying
mathematical problems, for instance, when double integrating accelerations to
get positions [WRYS15].
Data-driven machine learning models have become the standard in image analy-
sis [MCC+19, AN19, Edi21], beating the classical image analysis methods, thus
showing the benefits of data-driven methods.
Work using data-driven machine learning for condition monitoring of tracks has
proposed using on-board cameras to monitor the tracks [MR17] or using the
dynamics with a binary classification of the track as good or bad [dRKQ+21,
Tsu19, YSLL20]. However, these methods have downsides, as video analysis can
be computationally heavy [MR17], and classification techniques provide little
information regarding the severity of the irregularities.
[HYY+22] shows that the vertical irregularities can be accurately estimated
using a data-driven deep learning approach.
However, the proposed system
only predicts the vertical irregularities and makes no predictions regarding the
lateral irregularities.
[LHW21], utilize autoencoders to compress a segment
of irregularity data, such that they, rather than predicting the irregularities,
predict the compressed irregularities. This method gives excellent results, but
they use simulated data and focus on the longitudinal level, with the longitudinal
level as described in [LDGJ17].
Work in related areas, such as monitoring road quality, has shown promising
results using data-driven methods to process large amounts of data to give
frequent and reasonable estimates of road quality [VMT19].
==PAGE:18==
4
Introduction
==PAGE:19==
Chapter 2
Vehicle Modelling and the
Inverse Problem
Before getting into the prediction task, it is essential to understand the problem
at hand. It is entirely possible when using a data-driven method to predict A
from B without prior knowledge, but it can help to understand what A and B
are. This chapter will therefore present the problem in detail, such as where it
comes from and why it is relevant to use a data-driven approach.
The material is unless otherwise stated, based on previous work by the author
[PAD19, PD20, Ple21], the work by Lasse Engbo in his Master’s thesis [Eng01],
and conversations with Hans True [Tru22].
The problem deals with how a railway vehicle’s dynamics link to the rails’ irreg-
ularities. The first section will explain what is meant by rail irregularities. The
subsequent sections give an overview of a railway vehicle, define the dynamics
and motions, present how to model the dynamics using Newton-Euler equations,
and use said model to solve the dynamics numerically. With this in mind, the
last sections will pose the problem as an inverse problem of H. Here H maps
irregularities to dynamics. The focus is then on issues regarding the existence
of the inverse function H−1, and lastly, why data-driven methods are beneficial
and how a good solution is defined.
==PAGE:20==
6
Vehicle Modelling and the Inverse Problem
2.1
Rail irregularities
Before getting into modelling details, it is necessary to understand what is be-
ing modelled. I.e. what tracks, rails and railway vehicles are. Here the first
distinction is between tracks and rails. Rails refer to the metal rails the train
is in contact with, while tracks refer to everything built on the ground, such as
the rails, ballast and sleepers.
The rail irregularities describe how the position of the two rails deviates from
their nominal, i.e. intended, positions, as depicted in Fig. 2.1. The irregularities
consist of the lateral and vertical deviation for the two rails. The effect of rail
irregularities on railway vehicles’ dynamics is analogous to the effect of poor
road quality on cars’ and trucks’ dynamics. So if the irregularities have a high
variance, the railway vehicle will shake a lot, similar to a car’s rumble on a
bumpy road.

2.2
A railway vehicle
This project works with four-axle railway vehicles, as depicted in Fig. 2.2. Such
a system consists of a car body, two bogies connected to the car body through
the secondary suspension system, and four-wheel sets, two on each bogie. The
wheel sets are connected to the bogies through the primary suspension system,
as depicted in Fig. 2.3 and 2.4. The crosses mark the centre of mass for each
component.
Two different types of railway vehicles are considered in this project. The data
used later to solve the problem of interest comes from an ETR500 passenger
train. However, the mathematical model implemented at DTU Compute uses
a bogie model called Cooperrider’s bogie. The mathematical model uses this
bogie model because it is easier to model, as the springs and dampeners act
linearly, and the system’s dynamics are well understood.
The mathematical
model used in this project originates from the work done by Lasse Engbo in his
Master’s Thesis [Eng01], which was extended by the author et al. to allow for
==PAGE:22==

real track data [PAD19, PD20, Ple21].
However, the model has to be further updated to enable curved tracks, as the
old model only worked for straight tracks. Furthermore, a significant amount
of time was dedicated to updating the code base to utilize modern software for
scientific computing, such as Julia. The four main benefits of the transition from
C++ to Julia will be briefly mentioned. As this is slightly outside the focus of
this project, this will be a brief overview of the significant changes.
Firstly, to numerically solve the system’s dynamics, it is necessary to solve a
system of ordinary differential equations, ODEs, as described later in Sec. 2.4,
requiring an ODE solver. There are dozens of possible solvers available with
various benefits, and the choice of solver needs to be made based on the problem.
Having the model implemented in C++ makes it necessary to implement each
desired method or find a library with some of these implemented. However, these
implementations may not always be optimal and may require using different
interfaces, thus making comparisons/tests cumbersome. On the contrary, Julia
has many ODE solvers optimally or nearly optimally implemented, all accessed
through a standard interface. Quick access to many solvers makes it easy to
find a suitable solver for the problem and quickly test new solvers. The original
code used an explicit Runge-Kutta of order 5; however, explicit Runge-Kutta
methods are not well suited for stiff problems. The change to Julia allows for
easy conversion to a stiff ODE solver, which is relevant as the system of ODEs
modelling the vehicle dynamics is stiff [Eng01].
Secondly, similar to the previous point, updating the code becomes much more
manageable, partly due to a clean-up of the original code. However, it is also
because parts such as the ODE solver can be easily changed following any model
==PAGE:23==
2.3 Dynamics and motions of interest
9
modifications which change the system significantly. The code can also easily
be updated to run on a GPU if future work requires it.
Thirdly, Julia is a general-purpose programming language; however, it is spe-
cialized in numerical analysis and scientific computing. Besides a wide selection
of ODE solvers, Julia has many tools enabling various model analyses. These
tools will not be used in this project as they are outside the scope, e.g. calcu-
lating forward sensitivities, or they are beyond the scope, e.g. using data-driven
machine learning to align the model with real-world data.
Fourthly, this update also required a review of the code in terms of runtime, as
Julia and C++ are significantly different in how they achieve high-performance
code. The runtime is, therefore, more than ten times faster while being at least
as accurate. The performance increase is achieved by, amongst other changes,
adding the option to compute the Jacobian of the system, as many stiff ODE
solvers require it.
The two systems, the ETR500 passenger train and the mathematical model
differ slightly in the internal dynamics regarding the springs and dampeners.
However, the motions of interest, defined in the next section, are still the same.
As such, the output from the models can be used in similar circumstances.
Besides adding curved tracks to the mathematical model, the vehicle parameters
are updated from the ones given in [Eng01] to parameter values for an ETR500
passenger train with some modifications, as discussed in Sec. 2.4.3. The models
are thus slightly different; however, the dynamics should be similar [Bru22], and
the models can be used in conjunction, as discussed in Sec. 2.6.1.
When modelling the dynamics of a vehicle, it is important to consider the refer-
ence frame measurements are taken in relation to. Take, for instance, a person
sitting on a chair. That person is stationary with respect to the ground; how-
ever, they move at around 29.78 km/s with respect to the sun. Therefore, when
constructing a complete railway vehicle model, it is necessary to define multiple
coordinate systems and transfer forces between these. However, the scope of
this project is not to develop a complete model. As such, only a single coordi-
nate system is used that moves with the railway vehicle at a velocity V (t) and
follows the nominal track centreline, as indicated earlier in Fig. 2.1.
2.3
Dynamics and motions of interest
Now that a vehicle has been presented, it is necessary to understand which mo-
tions are relevant. The vehicles are modelled as rigid bodies connected through

the suspension systems. A depiction of the vehicle can be seen in Fig. 2.5. There
are three axes of motion, as well as rotations around these axes. Let the roll φ,
pitch χ, and yaw ψ denote the rotations around the x, y, and z axes, cf. Tab.
2.1. The dynamics then refers to the position, velocity and acceleration, angle,
angular velocity and angular acceleration of the components for the motions of
interest.
Rather than measuring the rotations of, e.g. a wheel set directly, another option
is to measure the accelerations at the axle boxes for the wheel set and determine
the roll, pitch, and yaw using trigonometry. This method is how the ETR500
passenger train handles the data, while the mathematical model uses the angles,
which makes it easier to compute the forces and torques acting on the bodies.
Not all motions are relevant for all bodies. For instance, the longitudinal position
of any component is irrelevant. The wheel sets’ motions of interest are the lateral
and vertical positions and the yaw angle. All motions except the longitudinal
positions are relevant for the bogies, i.e., the lateral and vertical positions and
the yaw, roll, and pitch angles. For the car body, the only motion modelled is the
roll angle. The dynamics are the various components’ accelerations, velocities,
and positions, cf. Tab. 2.2.
Additionally, to make computations easier, the model only includes the front
half of the car body, i.e. the code only models the motions for the front bogie

Vehicle Modelling and the Inverse Problem
2.4
Solving the system numerically
The purpose of vehicle modelling is to find the components’ dynamics over
time, for instance, when the vehicle is driving on a disturbed track. However,
the above description did not provide details regarding how the model is found.
Therefore, the purpose of this section is to present a vehicle model constructed
using Newton-Euler equations. Using this model, along with the fact that in-
tegration gives the velocity and position from the acceleration, the dynamics
can be found numerically by solving a system of ordinary differential equations,
ODEs, i.e. simulating the system.
The masses of the considered objects are constant, so the net force on an object
is equal to its mass times the acceleration of the object. Thus, given the net
force on the object, its acceleration can be found. Now Newton’s second law
comes into effect. After computing all forces in the directions of motion, then
the net force is just the sum of these.
This section will, therefore, first give an overview of the relevant forces acting
upon the components and how these give a system of ODEs. Afterwards, a
section focuses on how to do the simulation and modifications from the original
model [Eng01]. The motivation for these modifications is that the simulation
should reflect the dynamics of an ETR500 passenger train more closely. Thus,
data-driven methods can better use the two systems in conjunction.
2.4.1
Forces in the system
This part aims to present the model in terms of the Newton-Euler equations
so that the reader understands the origin of the forces influencing the system’s
dynamics. However, the project’s scope is not to construct a new railway vehicle
model, so parts, such as wheel-rail interactions, will only be briefly presented.
Any force acting upon a component can be grouped into external or internal
system forces. The former are forces from the environment, such as the tracks,
and most importantly rail irregularities, and forces from driving in a curve or
from gravity. The latter refers to forces from the springs and dampeners caused
by the relative displacements of the components in the system. From this and
the above, the net force on an object is given by,
F net = F internal + F external.
(2.1)
The system is big, so it is only written once in (2.2)-(2.15).
For a detailed
derivation of the internal and wheel-rail contact forces, the reader is referred
==PAGE:27==
2.4 Solving the system numerically
13
to [Eng01]. For an in-depth derivation of the forces arising from driving in a
canted curve, the reader is referred to [Big11, PH02, HL04].
It is necessary to consider the primary and secondary suspension systems to find
the system’s internal forces. These were depicted for the Cooperrider’s bogie
in Fig. 2.3 and 2.4. From the figures, the forces, as well as the arms when
considering torques, can be determined. This part is relatively straightforward
as the springs and dampeners are linear, and the forces and the torques are
independent.
Then the attention needs to be moved to the external forces, of which there
are three in the model. The forces from rail-wheel interaction are denoted as
contact forces. Then there are forces from driving in a curve, the centrifugal
force, and forces from gravity. The latter is caused by the tracks having a cant,
or incline, which is declining towards the centre of the curve such that the ride
comfort and safety are improved. The cant is an angle θ, and a visualization of
the cant is seen in Fig. 2.6.

Vehicle Modelling and the Inverse Problem
The contact forces, creepage and normal forces, occur due to the wheels pushing
down onto the rails and shear forces between the wheel and rails. It is a com-
plicated part of the system, which, for instance, depends on the wheel and the
rail profile. Also, the wheel sets have a flange, leading to non-linearities when
the lateral displacements are large. Modelling all this is a significant part of
what makes railway modelling a difficult problem, and an entire chapter could
be written as to how it is done. For the problem at hand, it is not necessary
to have in-depth knowledge of this part of the problem, and the estimation of
the forces will be treated as a black box. The creepage forces are given by Fi,j,k
and the normal forces are given by Ni,j,k, where i is the rail (left: l, right: r),
j is the dimension (longitudinal: x, lateral: y, vertical: z), and k is the wheel
set (front: 1, rear: 2). I.e. Nl,y,1 is the lateral component of the normal force
at the left rail on the front wheel set.
The rail-wheel contact forces only affect the wheel sets, while the forces from
driving in a curve and the forces from gravity affect the lateral and vertical
positions of the bogie and the wheel sets.
Using the internal and external forces and the notation defined in Tab. 2.3 along
with the notation ˙q = dq

2.4.2
The railway vehicle as a system of ordinary differ-
ential equations
The previous subsection stated the system’s accelerations or net forces. As the
accelerations are the second derivative of the position, then the above can be
seen as a system of second-order differential equations. Such a system can be
turned into a system of first-order differential equations.
However, the system is quite complex, so it is not feasible to find an analytical
solution to the system. Nevertheless, a numerical solution can still be found

Note that the vehicle’s position is part of the system, as the velocity, V (t), is not
constant, nor is it quasi-constant (constant acceleration or deacceleration). This
addition is also new from the original mathematical model. Ii,j is the moment
of inertia where i is the component (wheel set: w, bogie: b, car body c) and j
is the dimension (longitudinal: x, lateral: y, vertical: z).
Some ODE solvers require the Jacobian, i.e. the derivative of each expression
above w.r.t.
the variables ui.
Because most variables only occur in a few
expressions, e.g. u1 the front wheel set’s lateral position appears only in the
expression for ˙u2, ˙u10, ˙u12 and ˙u14, then the Jacobian is relatively sparse. The
ODE solvers can estimate it using finite differencing, but it is much faster and
more accurate to write up the analytical Jacobian.
The sparsity pattern is
shown in Fig. 2.7, and the equations can be seen in Appendix F as part of the
code for the railway vehicle ODE.
2.4.3
Simulating the system
When the numerical solution is calculated, it is necessary to initialize the sys-
tem, and as no information is known regarding this, a random state vector is
used. Except that the position of the vehicle, u31, is set to 10 m, ensuring the
input data is read correctly. Given the initial conditions, it is also necessary to
initialize some parameters for the rail-wheel forces.
The "system" will refer to the abstract object representing the railway vehicle.
The vector of variables ui defined in the previous chapter is the system’s state,
and the problem is finding the state vector over time. The system also contains
all other information regarding the vehicle, such as the last known creepage
forces, the previous state of the system, and rail data.
A few data sources are required to solve the problem. Firstly, the system requires
a data file, pm17mm_b.dat, with information regarding the wheel-rail contact.
Secondly, the system needs to load the parameters of the railway vehicle, such
==PAGE:33==
2.4 Solving the system numerically
19

as the spring and dampener constants and the weights and moments of inertia.
Thirdly, the system needs to load the track data which consist of the rails’
lateral and vertical irregularities, the velocity of the vehicle at a given position,
the curvature of the track and the cant.
It is necessary to have vehicle parameters to simulate the system, and the sim-
ulation code will use parameters from the ETR500 passenger train when these
overlap with the parameters in the mathematical model. I.e. weights, moments
of inertia and dimensions are all from the ETR500, while spring and damp-
ener constants use the ETR500 values when possible. However, as the ETR500
passenger train contains a couple of strong springs and dampeners not in the
mathematical model, it is necessary to modify the values that can be used. The
missing springs are around an order of magnitude stronger than those present,
so all values are increased at least an order of magnitude to keep the train on
the tracks. The simulation should then be able to mimic the dynamics of an
==PAGE:34==
20
Vehicle Modelling and the Inverse Problem
ETR500 passenger train [Bru22, Tru22]. Nevertheless, the simulation will still
differ from the actual dynamics of an ETR500 passenger train. But now also
from the dynamics of the Cooperrider’s bogie model in [Eng01].
In Fig. 2.8, a flowchart of the code has been shown.

2.4.4
Example of simulated dynamics
The presented system can simulate driving on the real track data. The data will
be explained in detail in the next chapter, but it contains the rail irregularities
divided into three disjoint wavelength bands. Hence, these need to be added
together to get time-series of the irregularities. Despite the modifications of the
spring and dampeners, the system still derails. However, by downscaling the
irregularities, this can be alleviated.
With this in mind, the simulation can be done, and a segment is seen in the
figure below, Fig. 2.9. The segment is chosen as it shows how the wheel set
generally follows the rail irregularities. However, from 13.7 to 13.9 km, the wheel
set oscillates even though the rails are straight. The area shows that the problem
cannot be accurately solved by merely considering the current dynamics, as the
dynamics may have been influenced by earlier irregularities.
It can also be noted from the figure that the system is sensitive to irregularities,
and small irregularities can result in large displacements of the wheel set.

2.4.5
Transforming data formats
The format of the data from the simulation needs to be modified before it
can be used. This reformatting requires transforming some coordinate systems
and using the previously mentioned linearizations. The linearizations make the
transformations of acceleration much simpler. These transformations have been
dealt with in great detail in previous work, and the full system can be found
in the code base in Appendix F. An example is that the lateral accelerations of
axle boxes 1 and 2 are both just the lateral acceleration of the front wheel set.
2.5
Posing the problem as an inverse problem
This section will present the problem of predicting the irregularities from the
dynamics as an inverse problem. The forward problem is the above-described
vehicle modelling.
==PAGE:36==
22
Vehicle Modelling and the Inverse Problem
2.5.1
System as a transfer function H
It is necessary first to have a forward problem to have an inverse problem.
However, this was described above as taking the rail irregularities and finding
the dynamics of a railway vehicle.
Hence, the system of interest, a railway
vehicle driving on rails with irregularities, can be seen as a mapping H taking
the track geometry as input and mapping it to the vehicle dynamics.
This
mapping has been depicted in Fig. 2.10.
Dynamics
H
Irregularities
Input
Output
Parameters
Wheel and rail profile
Vehicle parameters
Figure 2.10: Figure of the system H taking the irregularities as input and
outputting the dynamics. Secondary inputs such as the velocity,
curvature, and vehicle parameters (e.g.
spring constants and
dampening coefficients) are denoted as the parameters.
The specific problem solved in this project is to find, or rather approximate, the
inverse mapping H−1 that maps the vehicle dynamics to the track geometry,
specifically the rail irregularities. However, it is essential to remember that this
mapping is vehicle specific. Hence, using the approximated H−1 on dynamics
from a new vehicle may give incorrect results. This issue will be discussed in
more detail in Sec. 2.6.1.
2.5.2
Existence of the inverse H−1 – feasibility
An important aspect to consider is whether the mapping exists or not.
For
example, one could generate random samples of independent and identically
distributed data and try to learn a mapping from input to output. However,
while the models may work well on the data they see, they would generalize
poorly. I.e. predictions for unseen data would deviate significantly from the
==PAGE:37==
2.6 Why using data-driven methods?
23
intended output values for said data. This issue is because there is no actual
relationship or mapping from one to the other.
Previous work by the author et al.
in [PAD19, PD20] and by Lasse Engbo
in [Eng01] has shown that the system H is not an injective mapping. If the
irregularities follow a sine curve with a specific frequency, then the dynamics
are not affected, i.e. the state of the system is constantly zero except for the
position.
Hence, multiple inputs, i.e.
rail irregularities, give the same zero
solution, the system state over time. So since H is not an injective mapping,
then it is known from functional analysis that the inverse does not exist.
This result could imply that the task at hand is not solvable. However, this
fails to consider how often the above problem happens and how likely it is
to occur in real-world data.
As mentioned, the behaviour was observed for
irregularities forming perfect sine waves with precise frequencies. However, this
type of irregularity will never occur, as there will always be at least some amount
of noise. Previous work has also shown that if slight noise is added to the sine
curve, the dynamics are also affected by the sine wave.
If the sine waves of the specific frequencies are removed from the input space,
then the inverse may exist. Alternatively, at least a function that almost always
works can be constructed, and thus said function can be approximated.
2.6
Why using data-driven methods?
Before using a method, it is relevant to consider if it is even relevant.
As
discussed in the literature review, this problem is not new, and mapping the
dynamics to geometry is also not a new idea. However, many of the mathemat-
ical tools available are not usable for systems of this size, or detailed analytical
work is needed each time the system changes slightly.
The models are also
only as good as the knowledge of the system, and building a model which con-
tains everything is infeasible. However, a data-driven method does not require
a deep understanding of the system or how the components interact. Instead,
the method learns which features and feature transformations are relevant from
the data. This information extraction is especially the case when using complex
models such as a convolutional neural network, where the model can learn com-
plex feature transformations. For instance, if it is relevant to do a short-time
Fourier transformation, STFT, then the model can learn this automatically and
use the STFT to make predictions.
==PAGE:38==
24
Vehicle Modelling and the Inverse Problem
2.6.1
Limitations of data-driven methods
In extension to the previous section, it is crucial to be aware of the limitations
of a data-driven method.
For example, utilizing a data-driven approach to
approximate the inverse mapping H−1 implies that the inverse function is learnt
from data. Thus, the quality of the function approximation is only as good as
the data quality and quantity. Notably, if there are data issues, which are pretty
standard in real-world data [WW22], then the models produced from the data-
driven methods are rubbish. Even if the data issues are not severe, the model
may still suffer from poor quality or insufficient data.
A data-driven approach of the type done in this project also implies that the
H−1 mappings implicitly contain all system information. I.e. the results for
the mapping are specific to the tracks the data is generated from, and the
vehicle used to measure the dynamics. Some of these things may only have
a minor impact. For instance, fitting to Italian track data means the model
may not be as accurate on German or French track data if the tracks differ
significantly. Somewhat more seriously is that the mappings are also specific
to the railway vehicle used. Not just the specific train model, which here is an
ETR500 passenger train, but also the specific train used for the measurements.
I.e. if another ETR500 train set is used, then the tiny variations in, for instance,
the spring constants, measurement locations, and weight distribution can affect
the mapping. Also, changes in other less apparent conditions, such as weather
conditions, train operator, and the number of cars in the train set, can affect
the results.
One of the easiest ways to determine whether any of these are impactful would
be to get new data to test under a change of these conditions and, if necessary,
add data from various conditions to the fitting process. However, it may not
be all bad. Even if the method learns the system’s parameters, such as the
component weights, the function approximations for two different systems may
still be close in the space of function approximations. Hence, updating to a new
system might be relatively easy.
2.7
Example of an inverse problem – Kepler’s law
Some methods used in this project may require one or two examples from time
to time. For this purpose, Kepler’s laws will be used, particularly the third law,
which states that the square of the orbital period, T 2, is proportional to the
cube of the orbital radius, r3, [Wik22c], i.e. T 2 = ar3 where a ∈R. For the
==PAGE:39==
2.8 Models with current or historical and future data
25
current example, let the forward problem be to get the radius from the orbital
period. I.e. there is some known process which returns the orbital period when
inputting the radius.
The inverse problem would then be to reverse this and get the radius when
inputting the orbital period, so given f(r) = T, the goal is to find f −1(T) = r.
Since f is a simple expression, this is straightforward with algebra. However, if
the mapping was very complex, or no expression like the one above existed, the
problem would get much more complicated and require other methods, such as
data-driven machine learning, to solve.
2.8
Models with current or historical and future
data
When using prediction methods, such as machine learning, it is necessary to
consider what data is available and how it can be used. If one wishes to predict
the future price of a stock, then the only available data is current or past market
data.
The machine learning models considered in this project can be placed in two
categories: point-to-point models or time-series to time-series models. The for-
mer are models that take in the dynamics at a given point in time and predict
the geometry at that point. These are the classic models presented in Chap. 4.
While the latter are models that take time-series of the dynamics and predict
corresponding time-series of the irregularities, these models will be the convo-
lutional neural networks presented in Chap. 5. It is not an issue to have past
and future information about the dynamics, as the prediction task can be done
after all necessary data has been collected. Specifically, a model may, on the
high-end, require one kilometre of data before and after a point to make predic-
tions for said point. With vehicles driving at >200 km/h, this would imply an
18 seconds delay on the predictions, which is not an issue.
2.9
Target precision of models
After getting a model, or function approximator of H−1, which makes predic-
tions, then it is necessary to have a benchmark to determine when a model is
"good enough". This section will focus on how benchmarks (i)-(iii) are chosen
for all the models.
==PAGE:40==
26
Vehicle Modelling and the Inverse Problem
As will be shown in Sec. 3.5, the numerical precision allows for a prediction
accuracy of around 10−6. All the predictions are in millimetres, so this would
mean an accuracy of 1 nm, far lower than what is practically needed. Rather
than using this limit, the EN:13848-5 standard [Rai17] will be utilized. This doc-
ument contains operating limits for the track measurements at various speeds.
The strictest limits are deviations of 1 mm for 100 m running means and stan-
dard deviations. However, the deviations can be larger depending on the wave-
length band and measurements. E.g. for isolated defects in the track gauge, the
strictest is -5 mm, while the same speed allows isolated defects of +35 mm in
the track gauge.
The models’ prediction errors, the differences between the models’ predictions
and the actual value, will have to be low enough that it can be determined if
the operating limits are exceeded. Therefore, a benchmark of 0.1 mm for the
average unsigned error will be chosen [Tru22, Bru22]. Ideally, all predictions
should be close enough that the model can be used in connection with the
operating limit. Hence, a second and third benchmark is that the 95 % quantile
and the maximum of the unsigned errors are below 0.5 mm. These benchmarks
are higher, as having the maximum unsigned error less than x mm is much
stricter than having the mean unsigned error less than x mm.
Note that the limit values in [Rai17] are only specified to the nearest millimetre.
Hence, having a maximum unsigned error of less than 0.5 mm would imply that
no incorrect conclusions are drawn from rounding errors.
The three benchmarks used for all the machine learning models are:
(i) 0.1 mm for the mean of the unsigned errors,
(ii) 0.5 mm for the 95 % quantile of the unsigned errors,
(iii) 0.5 mm for the maximum of the unsigned errors.
If a model can pass all of these (or some), then said model will be denoted as
(partially) solving the problem. As will be discussed later in Sec. 4.11, the
following benchmarks are posed for the convolutional neural networks to be
satisfying.
(iv) 0.33 mm for the mean of the unsigned errors,
(v) 0.87 mm for the 95 % quantile of the unsigned errors,
(vi) 2.94 mm for the maximum of the unsigned errors.
==PAGE:41==
2.9 Target precision of models
27
[KYT02] gives error ranges that would accept maximum unsigned errors less
than 4 mm and an ME around 1 mm, while [HYY+22] sets a benchmark of 0.25
mm and 0.45 mm of the ME in respectively the wavebands [3 m, 42 m] and [42
m, 120 m]. These benchmarks are either not as strict or comparable to (iv)-(vi)
above. Hence, the above benchmarks for a satisfying neural network also work
as benchmarks for state-of-the-art solutions.
==PAGE:42==
28
Vehicle Modelling and the Inverse Problem
==PAGE:43==
Chapter 3
Data engineering
The previous chapter introduced a vehicle model and described how H is a
function mapping the irregularities to the dynamics of the railway vehicle. The
problem of predicting the irregularities from the dynamics is then defined as
approximating the inverse function, H−1.
Using a data-driven approach to approximate an inverse function H−1 requires,
as the term suggests, data. This chapter focuses on presenting the data provided
by Stefano Bruni, exploring the features of the data, and preparing the data
for later utilization in machine learning models. The exploration includes an
overview of any data issues and the data modifications done because of these
issues.
3.1
Overview of the data
The inverse function takes in measurements of the dynamics and outputs the
irregularities. The dynamics consist of measurements of the various components’
accelerations. The accelerations are measured at the eight axle boxes, on the
bogies at the contact points with the eight axle boxes, and on the car body
at the two bogies; see Fig. 3.1 for a depiction of the measurement locations.
==PAGE:44==
30
Data engineering

Figure 3.1: Measurement locations of the dynamics.

placement of the accelerometers on the axle boxes, bogies and car
body.
Similarly, the irregularities of the rails are measured in the lateral and vertical
directions, after which the measurements are filtered into the three frequency
domains D1 (wavelengths in [3 m, 25 m]), D2 (wavelengths in [25 m, 70 m]),
and D3 (wavelengths in [70 m, 200 m]) using bandpass filters. The accelerations
are stored in a dataset denoted as the dynamics, while the irregularities are
stored in a dataset called the geometry. The dynamics dataset also contains the
velocity of the railway vehicle and the track curvature. The datasets provided
by Stefano Bruni contained additional features, but these were discarded as they
were not relevant for the task at hand [Bru22].
A sample in the dynamics dataset consists of the features summarized in Tab.
3.2. In contrast, a sample in the geometry data consists of the features summa-
rized in Tab. 3.1.
The datasets were sampled in different ways. The accelerations were sampled
with ≈1 kHz while the geometry was measured every 0.5 m.
However, the
accelerations were also augmented with measurements every 0.5 m to align the
datasets. This augmentation is crucial as the methods employed to approximate
the inverse function require that the input and output data, the dynamics and
geometry data, can be aligned.
The datasets are stored as two matrices, dynamics and geometry, where each
row is a sample, and rows are removed from dynamics s.t.
it also only has
samples every 0.5 m. The result is two matrices, each with 281,827 rows of real

3.2
Data correlation
After getting an overview of the data sources, it is beneficial to get a better feel
for the data by calculating some descriptive statistics and plotting the data; the
latter is done in the next section. These calculations give an overview of the data
distributions and how features are correlated. Also, they are an effective way to
discover data issues. For instance, in the provided data, the curvature was given
in both the dynamics and the geometry datasets; however, it was constantly zero
in the dynamics. This issue was fixed by copying the values from the geometry
dataset to the dynamics dataset. The curvature was afterwards dropped, along
with other unnecessary features, from the geometry dataset.
Firstly, the correlations between the features in the dynamics dataset will be ex-
amined. For this purpose, the correlations have been visualized in Fig. 3.2. The
values of correlations are the values of the standard correlation coefficient, also
known more specifically as the Pearson correlation coefficient, which measures
linear dependence between two variables. Based on the knowledge of the vehicle
dynamics discussed in the previous chapter, a strong correlation between, e.g.
axle box 1 and 2’s lateral accelerations is expected, as these showed strong linear
dependence (they are equal).
The features are generally weakly correlated. However, there are a few note-
worthy exceptions, namely between the ID pairs between 5, 9, 13 and 17, and
the pairs between 21, 25, 29 and 33. These are the vertical (Z) accelerations of
bogies A and B at axle boxes 1 to 8.
From the vehicle dynamics presented earlier, these correlations are not unex-
pected and are also grouped as expected. I.e. the accelerations measured on
bogie A are strongly correlated, while the correlations between pairs of accel-
erations measured at bogie A and accelerations measured at bogie B are much
smaller. It is not unexpected that there is some correlation between them as
they are connected through the suspensions and car body, but the correlation
was expected to be smaller. Also, the correlation between axle boxes 1 and 2’s
lateral accelerations (ID 2 and 6) is relatively small, which is unexpected given
the vehicle dynamics. This lack of correlation could hint at either data issues
or that the previously presented model is not sufficiently accurate.
==PAGE:48==
34
Data engineering
It is interesting to note that the curvature (ID 38) is mainly correlated with the
lateral accelerations of the car body at bogie A and B. The curvature, therefore,
has a direct impact on the accelerations of the car body. This correlation is
expected as the curvature directly impacts the moment along the longitudinal
axis (the roll moment) of the car body.
Additionally, it should be noted that while most coefficients are around the
level ≈10−2, they are still significant; using the Student’s t-distribution test for
Pearson correlation coefficients, the critical value is ≈2·10−3. So the dynamics
are almost all weakly correlated, which matches the knowledge that the system
is connected through rigid bodies, springs and dampeners. Nevertheless, there
are still components, such as axle boxes 1 and 2, where strong correlations would
have been expected.

==PAGE:49==
3.2 Data correlation
35
The purpose of this project is not to find relations between the dynamics but
rather to find a relation/mapping from the dynamics to the irregularities/track
geometry. Therefore, it is relevant to see how the dynamics correlate with the
geometry. A figure of these correlation coefficients can be seen in Fig. 3.3. From
the figure, it can be seen that only a few of the dynamics and geometry feature
pairs are highly linearly correlated. The lack of linear correlation between the
dynamics and the geometry does not make finding a mapping between the two
impossible. However, it does show that the problem will likely require more
than a simple linear regression model, which is already known in the literature
[Tru22]. Furthermore, the relations may be slightly more complicated than a
linear transform from one dynamics feature to a geometry feature.
This discussion approaches the area of feature engineering, where input features,
features in the dynamics dataset, have non-linear transformations applied to
them. These transformations could be any non-linear transformation, such as
polynomials, exponentiations, logarithms, sines, cosines, and combinations of
these. From this, the reader may notice that feature engineering is quite tricky
and requires prior knowledge of which transformations would be sensible to
perform. However, the primary attractor for using a data-driven approach is
that the models should discover sensible transformations from the data.

Data engineering
3.3
Visualizing the data
Before starting further on approximating the inverse function, a better grasp
of the data is needed. The last part showed some potential data issues, and
an efficient way to investigate this is to plot the data, which has the added
benefit of showing how the data varies over time.
Two figures are made by
plotting the geometry data in the time and time-frequency domains. Fig. 3.4
shows plots of the time-series of data, while Fig. 3.5 shows spectrograms of
the time-series. The spectrograms are of interest since a spectrogram shows the
dominant frequencies over time in a signal. Furthermore, it is often of interest
for geometry data which frequencies dominate the irregularities. Irregularities
with a very high frequency, and thus a very short wavelength << 1 m, are of
little interest as the dynamics are unaffected by them. Meanwhile, irregularities
with a medium wavelength, e.g. of 1 −200 m, are hugely important.
Based on the plots in the time-domain, Fig. 3.4, it is noticeable that the magni-
tudes in the D3 wavelength range are noticeably higher than for the two other
ranges, particularly for the vertical irregularities. Also, after the 100 km mark,
the variation increases in the D3 plots, which can also be seen in the spectro-
grams. This variation is crucial later when discussing how the data should be
handled when fitting models.
Other than this, there are no noticeable data
issues in the geometry data.
Then moving on to the dynamics, the features are grouped based on compo-
nent, axle box, bogie or car body, and direction, lateral (Y) or vertical (Z). The
plots of these features can be seen in Fig. 3.6-3.10. From Fig. 3.6 with the
lateral accelerations of the axle boxes, it seems there might be issues with the
accelerometer at axle boxes 5 and 6. The scale of these measurements is vastly
different from the other axle boxes – though axle boxes 3 and 4’s measurements
are also an order of magnitude lower than the measurements for axle boxes 1, 2,
7 and 8. The same issue seems to be prevalent at axle box 3 when measuring the
vertical accelerations, as seen in Fig. 3.7; the measurements are much smaller
than for the other axle boxes. Another figure with a 400 m segment is made, Fig.
3.11, to look further into this issue where the y-axis’s range is more appropriate
for the features. From this, it can be seen that the measurements of the lateral
accelerations at axle boxes 5 and 6 seem unnatural; there seem to be limits that
the measurements only rarely can exceed. Similarly, the measurements of the
vertical accelerations at axle box 3 also seem to be locally bound. The issue also
seems to be affecting axle boxes 3 and 4’s lateral accelerations; however, these
show it to a smaller extent. But, axle box 4’s measurements are centred at 1
rather than at 0, which is another potential data issue.
Furthermore, for axle box 2 and 6’s vertical accelerations, there seem to be some
outliers around respectively positions 75 km and 30 km, where a few measure-

issues with the faulty sensors, for axle 3’s vertical and axle boxes 3 to 6’s lateral
accelerations, will be ignored, i.e. no changes will be made to the data. The
sensible data modifications are to replace the faulty values with values that
are either zero or another constant value, e.g.
a mean of non-faulty values,
an interpolation of non-faulty values or similar. However, as there are no non-
faulty values, the result would be replacing every value with a constant or simply
dropping the features, as no information would then be provided by said feature.
However, there might be some signal/information regarding the irregularities
the models will be able to extract based on when the values are positive or
negative or below or above 1 for axle box 4’s lateral accelerations. The values
are, therefore, not modified 1. However, some machine learning methods can
have issues with data that contain features with values across several orders
of magnitude.
The data, therefore, need to be standardized, which will be
explained further in Sec. 3.8. Another issue was the outliers in the vertical
accelerations for axle boxes 2 and 6. These span short time intervals, so they
could be disregarded, i.e. zeroed.
Lastly, the dynamics also contain the velocity of the railway vehicle and the
track curvature. A plot of these has been shown in Fig. 3.12. The figure shows
that there do not appear to be any data issues for these features. However, it is
relevant for later that there are some variations in the data. For instance, the
initially low velocity and the dip in velocity around a position of 110-120 km.

modification was not possible due to time constraints, but it will be touched on in Sec. 7.2 as
future work.

3.4
Principal component analysis
It was previously tested to what extent the features in the dynamics dataset
are correlated, and the results indicated no high correlations. This test was
also partially done, as some machine learning models can exhibit irregular per-
formance when fitting with strongly correlated input features. Also, reducing
the number of features in the input can be beneficial in fitting a model faster,
as fewer parameters may be needed. These two issues are less relevant in this
project, as the first issue occurs more frequently for classical machine learning
models like linear regression, more details will follow later in Chap. 4, and the
latter issue is more relevant for very high dimensional input, where the number
of input features is at least in the hundreds or thousands.
The "big brother" of testing correlation coefficients is performing a principal
component analysis, PCA. The purpose of doing a PCA is to find a smaller
dimensional subspace that explains as much variation in the data as possible.
Rather than just testing pairs of features and calculating their correlation, a
PCA aims to describe one feature as a linear combination of other features.
The dynamics data contain 38 features, so the data live in a 38-dimensional
==PAGE:60==
46
Data engineering
space, and the goal would be to find a subspace of dimension k < 38. In a PCA
analysis, the mapping from the original space to the subspace must be linear.
With this restriction, one may show that performing a PCA amounts to doing
a singular value decomposition, SVD, of the data. When ordering the resulting
singular values from highest to smallest, then the first k components of the SVD
are the basis vectors for the optimal k dimensional subspace [HSM18].
If σi, i ∈{1, ..., 38} are the ordered singular values when doing a decomposition
of the dynamics dataset, then the variance explained by the k’th component is
vk and is calculated as
vk =
σk
P38
i=1 σi
,
(3.1)
and as the components are linearly independent, the variance explained is also
independent, i.e. the variance explained by the first k components is Vk and is
given by
Vk =
Pk
j=1 σj
P38
i=1 σi
.
(3.2)
After calculating the SVD of the dynamics data, the results have been visualized
in Fig. 3.13 by plotting the variation explained by the k’th component on the
left and by the first k components on the right using eq. 3.1 and 3.2. It can
be seen that 19, 25, and 30 components are needed to capture respectively 95
%, 99 %, and 99.9 % of the variation in the data. It is, therefore, possible to
slightly decrease the amount of input by compressing the data. However, after
some initial tests, there does not seem to be any benefit from compressing the
dynamics data.
3.5
Numerical precision of data
When handling data for any data-sensitive project, it is essential to consider
how to store the data. As all the data used in this project are continuous real
values, it is only necessary to consider how the values are stored as floats in
terms of precision. The issue occurs as computers work with finite precision,
so if this is not considered, then data and hence important information may be
lost involuntarily.
The measurements are performed with a precision of 10−4, and the largest values
are of the order of magnitude 102.
Therefore, to not lose any information,
it is necessary to store six significant digits.
There are two commonly used
types of floats, float32 and float64, which differ by how many bytes they use in
==PAGE:61==

marked. The singular values sort the components in descending

==PAGE:62==
48
Data engineering
memory, respectively, 4 and 8. Float32 has a precision of 2−24 ≈5.9605 · 10−8
for 1. I.e. when using float32, the smallest number strictly greater than 1 is
≈1.000000059605. The exponent is stored separately and can range from -126
to 127, which means that float32 can hold seven significant digits for numbers
ranging in magnitude from -126 to 127 [Wik22d]. Hence, no loss of precision
occurs when storing the numbers as float32.
The conversion from float64 to float32 is standard practice as the memory foot-
print is halved, and when working with big models, the training time is signif-
icantly shortened. This speedup is because computations on float32 are faster
due to the fewer bytes, and specialized hardware also has more processing power
for float32.
3.6
Data segmentation
The entire goal when approximating the inverse function H−1 is to give usable
predictions when the model is applied in the real world. However, when using a
data-driven approach to fit a function approximator, a machine learning model,
the models need some data to learn the mapping. The data used by the model
to learn the mapping will be called the training data. The model learns the
mapping from the dynamics to the geometry using the training data. Errors
made on the training data are, therefore, likely too optimistic compared to
what would be observed when applying the model in the real world. Hence, it is
necessary to mimic/simulate this real-world application. This simulation can be
done by withholding some data, called the test data. The test data cannot be
used to make any model decisions, such as tuning models and selecting between
multiple models.
The loss captures how well a model fits some data, and the model would likely
make some errors when it is used to make predictions. These errors are quanti-
fied to a single number by the loss – the loss is zero if the model fits the data
perfectly. The generalization loss is the expected loss when the model is used in
the real world. The purpose of the testing data is to estimate the generalization
loss of the final model.
The training and test data have natural roles in the scientific method. Con-
tinuing the example with Kepler’s law from the previous chapter, the training
data could be observational data from Earth, Mercury, Mars and Saturn. In
contrast, the test data would be data from Venus and Jupiter. Data used by
Kepler taken from [Wik22c] has been shown in Tab. 3.3 with the split. The
data will be used for examples in the next chapter. After developing a model, it
==PAGE:63==
3.6 Data segmentation
49
could then be tested on the data from Venus and Jupiter, but the model should
not be fitted to observations from these. Kepler’s laws fit the data better than
Copernicus’s heliocentric theory. Because of this, Kepler’s laws are "better"
than Copernicus’s heliocentric theory.
Split

Table 3.3: Data used by Kepler, taken from [Wik22c].
The problem is that the dynamics and geometry data needs to be divided into a
training and a testing set. The goal will be to ensure, using only the information
provided so far in this chapter, that both the training and testing data contain
the various kinds of dynamics and geometry pairs, or simply system behaviours,
that the data contain. An example, which may be easier to visualize, would
be the task of looking at pictures of faces and determining whether the person
is smiling or frowning. The data connected to the task contain many pictures
of people of all genders, ethnicities, and ages. The task is then to split the
training and testing data such that both sets contain pictures of people of all
genders, ethnicities, ages, and so forth. The model would exhibit poor real-
world performance if it were only trained on a single gender, age group and
ethnicity, as it then would be unable to learn which features are relevant across
ages, genders, and ethnicities. The task here is the same, but it is less clear
what distinguishes one part of the data from another.
Using the plots from earlier, particularly the plots of the velocity and curvature
and the spectrograms of the geometry data, the data is split into six segments.
The first, third and fifth segments are used as the test set, while the second,
fourth and sixth segments are used as the training set.
These regions are chosen with the intention that the test and training datasets
both should contain samples of a similar kind, and that the various kind of
system behaviour should be present in both.
Specifically, the data has 287,827 samples available in total. The 1st to 23,827th
sample, the 94,001st to 117,827th sample, and the 188,001st to 211,827th sample
are used as test data. Meanwhile, the training data consist of the 23,828th to
==PAGE:64==
50
Data engineering
94,000th sample, 117,828th to 188,000th sample, and 211,827th to 281,827th
sample. This segmentation has been visualized in Fig. 3.14, similar to Fig.
3.12, but the testing and training regions are highlighted.

3.7
Data formatting
The data must be formatted to make it usable for fitting machine learning
models. This requires two kinds of splits, one of which is used for the simple
models, which takes in a single point in the dynamics space and outputs a single
point in the geometry space. In contrast, the convolutional neural networks
require another data format in which the input is time-series of the dynamics,
and the model outputs time-series of the geometry.
Additionally, data-driven models contain parameters which need to be tuned,
but two models also need to be compared. However, as the comparisons should
==PAGE:65==
3.8 Standardization of data
51
ideally be made regarding the generalization loss, the training data is unfit, as
mentioned in the previous section. The model may overfit, so it is getting a low
training loss. However, the issue stems from the model learning the training
data and not the function it is supposed to approximate. This issue will be
discussed in-depth when fitting the models.
However, the testing data may also not be used, as it can only be used to
estimate the generalization loss of the final model reported in the conclusion.
The solution is to take some training data, call it the validation data, and use the
loss on the validation data as a better estimate of the generalization loss than
the training data provides. The validation data is not used to fit coefficients in
a model, but the validation data is used for making choices between models.
As shown in the previous section, the training data consists of three disjoint and
disconnected regions. These regions are each split into 70 segments, giving 210
segments of 1000 samples for the simple models. These 210 segments are then
divided between training and validation such that 30 % of the segments, =63
segments, are used for validation. The segments are pairs of matrices, where
each row is an observation of respectively dynamics and geometry/irregularities.
The matrices are then stacked such that there are matrices Atrain,Aval,ytrain
and yval with respectively the dynamics for training and validation, and the
irregularities for training and validation.
For the convolutional neural networks, the above data format is not applicable
as the matrices must be continuous track data, i.e. the columns must be time-
series. The validation data is extracted by taking 5 km of track data just before
or after a test region. This format gives six segments of validation data, which
are used for cross-validation, the result being that around 30 % of the data is
used for validation. The remaining data is kept as a list of pairs of matrices,
where each matrix is of continuous track data. I.e. the columns are time-series
of either dynamics or geometry.
The test data is split into three segments of 23,827 samples, and each segment
is just one of the test regions mentioned above. When computing metrics, the
computations are done across the errors for the three regions.
3.8
Standardization of data
Some models that will be utilized will require the data to have features of similar
magnitudes. From Fig. 3.4, it can be seen that this should not be an issue for the
output. However, as seen earlier, some features are affected by faulty sensors,
==PAGE:66==
52
Data engineering
which causes these features to be four-five magnitudes smaller than features
with non-faulty sensors.
This issue can be easily fixed by standardizing the
dynamics data, which means that the mean is subtracted, and the features are
scaled to have unit variance.
Specifically, the means and standard deviations of each column of the matrix
Atrain are calculated. Let µj and σj be respectively the mean and standard
deviation of the values in the j’th column of Atrain; i.e. if Atraini,j is the
element in the i’th row and j’th column then,
µj = 1
n
