The numerical issues incurred if the standardization is not done will be elabo-
rated upon in Sec. 4.4 after the linear model has been introduced.
3.9
Interpolated data
Initial tests indicate that the neural network models suffer from insufficient
data. Furthermore, a sample spacing of 0.5 m is longer than what was tested
beforehand, as previous tests on artificially generated data used a sample spacing
of 0.1 m.
As the dynamics are sampled with ≈1 kHz, it is given with an
irregular position spacing, which was down-sampled to a constant spacing of 0.5
m. Because of the extra data, the dynamics can be interpolated to a smaller
spacing of 1/6 m with high accuracy. Using a spacing of 1/6≈0.1667 m is still
larger than 99.99 % of the sample distances in the dynamics data, and because
of this, the interpolations should be very accurate. The interpolations are done
in MATLAB using splines.
The interpolated data will be used for the convolutional neural networks, as
they can efficiently change the sample spacing from input to output.
==PAGE:67==
Chapter 4
Classic machine learning
models
The previous chapter presented the data required to fit the data-driven machine
learning used in this project to approximate the inverse function H−1. This
chapter will then introduce machine learning and how to use it. This chapter
focuses on using classic machine learning methods, and the next chapter will
then focus on convolutional neural networks, which is a slightly more advanced
method.
The presentations of the methods draw largely on the books [HSM18, CK13]
unless otherwise stated in the text. The connection between machine learning
and function approximation is based on the material presented in the lectures
[Cev21a, Cev21e, Cev21f, Cev21g, Cev21i, Cev21j].
4.1
What is machine learning
Before anything else, the critical question is, what is machine learning? Machine
learning focuses on using data-driven algorithms to say something about data.
The computer or "machine" learns complex concepts by seeing many examples.
The idea is similar to a person learning the concept of a cup by being given
==PAGE:68==
54
Classic machine learning models
cups. After seeing and interacting with some cups, the concept of a cup begins
to settle in the person’s mind.
Machine learning is broadly separated into two categories, supervised and un-
supervised learning.
Supervised learning is likely the version people think of when imagining ma-
chine learning. Here, the purpose is to learn a mapping from input to labels.
An example of this is a child is shown 100 toys and given a label for each, such
as bunny, teddy bear, LEGO person, or car. Afterwards, the child is shown
a new toy and asked what it is. Other more technical examples are translat-
ing text from one language to another, speech-to-text, image classification, and
predicting the selling price of a house based on inputs such as the size of the
property and neighbourhood. Mathematically speaking, supervised learning is
about finding a mapping f : X 7→Y based on input data X ⊂X and output
data y ⊂Y such that f(X) is close to y. Sec. 4.2 defines what "close to" means
and how the mapping can be found.
Unsupervised learning methods aim to capture clusters in the data or learn new
data representations. An example of the former could be that a child is given a
box of 100 toys, and the child is then handed a new toy and asked: "Find five
toys similar to this". The 100 toys can form many clusters of similar toys, and
the task is then to identify the cluster to which the new toy belongs and extract
elements from it. An example of the latter is that a person deals with data
consisting of matrices of n by n elements, where all elements are equal. Hence,
if one matrix is of size 100 by 100 and all elements are 2, then representing
the matrix as the tuple (100,2) suffices for capturing all the information in the
matrix.
In abstract terms, this is what machine learning does. This project will use
supervised learning to learn the mapping, the inverse function H−1, from dy-
namics to track irregularities. The following parts will focus on how supervised
machine learning is done, i.e. how the computer learns the mapping.
4.2
Error measure and loss functions – how the
machine learns
The last section introduced machine learning and the end goal of supervised
machine learning. Nevertheless, it crucially left out how to find the mapping.
For this, it is necessary to get a bit more technical. Let ΩF be a function space,
i.e. a collection of available functions. Given some input data X and some labels
y, the goal is to find the best function, or mapping f ∈ΩF, of the input to the
==PAGE:69==
4.2 Error measure and loss functions – how the machine learns
55
labels. Best implies that some mappings are better than others, necessitating
a way to rank them. For the following, the focus will be on regression tasks,
which means that the labels y are continuous real values.
When f maps the input to predicted labels ˆy, these will naturally deviate from
the true labels y.
These deviations, or errors, can, for example, be due to
random noise, which cannot be modelled exactly, but it can also be because the
wrong function/mapping f is used.
If the predictions at time t are ˆyt with the true values being yt, Then the errors,
or residuals, at time t, ϵt, are given by:
ϵt = |ˆyt −yt|.
(4.1)
However, this has just changed the problem ever so slightly. The ranking of one
function over another is still not clear. Sure, if function f1 always has errors
that are twice as large as for function f2, then it is reasonable that function f1
is worse than function f2. Consider then function f3, which half the time makes
errors twice as large as function f1 and the other half of the time has zero errors;
is function f1 or function f3 then a better choice?
The problem mentioned above is that comparisons of models use many numbers,
which may be both positive and negative. It is easier to compare the functions
if only a single number is used for comparisons. This single number is the loss
mentioned in the previous chapter. A method to summarize the inaccuracies by
a function is thus needed, i.e. an error measure or a loss function as it is more
commonly known.
There are many choices, even for a simple problem. For instance, the loss might
weigh over-predictions, i.e. ϵt > 0, higher than underpredictions. Generally, the
loss can be constructed in many ways, as long as it gives a valid ranking of the
mappings, i.e. if the loss of function f1 is higher than the loss for function f2,
then function f2 should be better or more desirable for the task at hand. There
are also some practical considerations, making some loss functions better than
others. It should be continuous and all most always differentiable, i.e. there
may be single points where the gradient is ill-defined such as for |x|, but these
can be handled using a sub-gradient method [Cev21f].
There are of course common choices, such as 1/(sample size) times the l1-norm
or the squared l2-norm of the errors. The latter is called the mean squared error,
MSE, i.e.
MSE(f) = MSE(f(X), y) = MSE(ˆy, y) = 1
n
n
X
t=1
(ˆyt −yt)2 = 1
n
n
X
t=1
ϵ2
t,
(4.2)
and will be used in this project. The data does not seem to contain any clear
outliers, which is important as the MSE is not robust to outliers.
Note that the mean squared error is not just 1/n times the squared error. The
==PAGE:70==
56
Classic machine learning models
squared error is usually defined as,
SE(f) = SE(ˆy, y) = 1
2
n
X
t=1
(ˆyt −yt)2 = 1
2
n
X
t=1
ϵ2
t.
(4.3)
The addition of the 1/2 factor makes the derivative slightly simpler.
The problem can now be formally stated as finding f ∗given as,
f ∗= argmin
f∈ΩF
MSE(f).
(4.4)
I.e. the problem is to find the function in the function space, which minimizes
the loss, the MSE. This statement could be implemented using a computer by
trying every f ∈ΩF. However, this last bit may sound easy, but the function
space may be large or infinite. For example, the functions f(x) = α+x, α ∈[0, 1]
may all be in the function space, which makes it infeasible to try every f ∈ΩF.
The idea for fixing the issue mentioned above is to restrict the function space.
Instead of having the function space,
ΩF = {α + x}α∈[0,1] ∪{exp(α · x)}α∈[0,10] ∪{sin(α · x)}α∈[0,10],
(4.5)
consider the function space ΩF = {α+x}α∈R. Then rather than minimizing over
functions, the minimization is over the parameter α, or the model coefficient.
Then one may utilize optimization theory to speed up the fitting process.
Continuing the earlier example with Kepler’s laws from the previous chapters,
specifically with Kepler’s third law, which states T 2 ∝a3, the problem could be
stated as finding β such that T 2 = βa3.
The following parts will present various models and how they are fitted to the
data. Note that these models will be point-to-point models, which means they
take a point in the dynamics space as input and outputs a point in the geometry
space. The next chapter will focus on convolutional neural networks, which take
time-series of the dynamics as the input and output time-series of the geometry.
The structure is meant to start with a simple model and then add concepts
and ideas to make it more advanced. The models in the upcoming sections are
using the matrices Atrain, Aval, ytrain and yval as respectively the training
and validation input, and training and validation output (labels) as explained
in Sec. 3.7.
==PAGE:71==
4.3 The constant model
57
4.3
The constant model
The simplest model imaginable is to output the same constant value regardless
of the input. This model can be expressed as:
ˆyt = c,
(4.6)
where c ∈Rm.
When using the MSE as the loss function, the constant model outputs the mean
of each geometry feature in the training data.
Hence, the i’th value of c is
the mean of the i’th column of ytrain. So when given a new data point to
predict, the constant model says the lateral irregularity of the left rail in the
D1 wavelength is the mean of the lateral irregularities of the left rail in the D1
wavelength in the training data.
4.4
Linear model
A slightly more complex model is to add a linear transformation from the dy-
namics space to the geometry space, which gives an affine transformation of the
input to the output. This method is the linear model, which can be written as:
ˆyt = xtC + b,
(4.7)
where C ∈Rk×m and b ∈Rm. As mentioned, this is an affine transformation,
not a linear one, so why is it called a linear model? By augmenting the vector
xt with a 1, the transformation can be written as
ˆyt = xtC,
(4.8)
and it is a linear transformation from the new feature space to the geometry
space.
To return to the example with Kepler’s third law, one can pose the problem as
solving a linear model where xt is the 1d vector (a3) and yt is the 1d vector (T 2).
One would then stack the xt’s and yt’s into vectors X and y. Finding the model
coefficients, which here is the earlier mentioned coefficient β, by minimizing the
MSE, can be done by computing the expression
C = (XT X)−1XT y.
(4.9)
==PAGE:72==
58
Classic machine learning models

in place of the validation loss), one would get the following

The coefficient β is close to the theoretical value of 133,404.48. Using the test
data and β = 134, 591.72 the MSE loss is 2.343 · 1010. This value might seem
high, but the loss is a relative scale. It can be used to rank models, but it is
difficult to say a model is twice as good because the loss is halved. However,
with a loss such as the MSE, there is a possible interpretation; if the loss is
halved, the squared errors are half as big on average, so the errors are smaller
on average.
Rather than just augmenting xt with a one as before, one could augment with
any non-linear transformation, denoted as basis functions, of the input features.
However, when just considering polynomial transformations, the options grow
exponentially in the maximum degree. Therefore, this area is not pursued due
to a lack of prior knowledge that would help narrow down which basis functions
to use.

4.5
Under and overfitting – the need for regular-
ization
The last section brought up overfitting but did not explain what it is.
The
concept is relatively simple.
Sometimes, the model becomes too big or too
"free" for the data available. When this happens, the model can adapt to the
training data too well, to the point that it also captures the noise in the data. A
slightly more clear-cut example than the previous one is shown below, Fig. 4.1.
A data set of six samples are generated from the function f(x) = 5+2·x−0.5·x2
and standard normal i.i.d. noise is added to the samples. Three functions are

The model should be made big or free enough to learn the trends in the data to
avoid underfitting, but then one runs into the risk of overfitting. To deal with
overfitting, one can utilize regularization, a collection of methods used to force
==PAGE:75==
4.6 Norm of weights
61
simpler solutions that should generalize to new data better.
Regularization comes in two forms, explicit and implicit. The first is where the
objective is modified, e.g. by introducing priors or constraints. Meanwhile, the
latter is other forms such as early stopping, using methods robust to outliers,
and choice of optimization algorithm.
Regularization usually increases the training loss, but the validation loss is im-
proved, and more importantly, the generalization loss is improved.
4.6
Norm of weights
One of the simplest regularization techniques is penalizing the weights’ norm.
Specifically, there are two common ways, penalize the l1 or l2 norm of the model
coefficients, and if the model coefficients are collected in the object C then l1
and l2 regularization amounts to adding, respectively, λ∥C∥1 and λ∥C∥2
2 to the
loss function. The former results in sparse solutions [Cev21e] while the latter is
useful for multicollinearity between the input attributes [He20]. Let MSE(C)
be the MSE when using the model f with coefficients C to make predictions
on the training data.
When using the l2 norm regularization, the objective
becomes,
min
C MSE(C) + λ∥C∥2
2.
(4.11)
Solving the problem eq. (4.11) is known in the literature as ridge regression.
One of the advantages of l2- over l1 regularization is that it has an analytical
solution similar to eq. (4.9) namely
C = (XT X + λI)−1XT y,
(4.12)
where I is the identity matrix of appropriate size. Solving the previous problem
using ridge regression requires selecting a good, ideally the optimal, value of λ.
The common way to do this is to fit the model to the data using a range of
candidates for λ. Then the value of λ yielding the lowest loss on a validation set
is used. In the above example, the validation set is the new data set. Doing this
yields the plot seen in Fig. 4.2. This figure shows that the training loss increases
drastically as λ increases. However, at a certain point, the regularization makes
the model generalize much better, around an optimal λ = 1.26.
An example using three of the λ values can then be seen in the figure below,
Fig. 4.3, where the effect of ridge regression can be seen. Using even a little
==PAGE:76==

normalization drastically decreases the variation seen before for f6. However,
the function still overfits to the data, but as λ increases further, the shape
becomes closer to the true solution. When λ is increased beyond the optimal
value, the fitted models become much worse.
When using a too high λ for
weight norm regularization, the solutions are biased towards mappings that are
too simple, such as the mean of the output. While if not enough regularization
is used, then the mapping has too much variance. This trade-off is known as
the bias-variance trade-off.
The terms bias and variance refer to the mapping, which can be seen as a
random variable in the data. I.e. if another data set were collected of the same
size, then the mapping would be slightly different.
Many models have hyperparameters that control various parts of the model or
the problem. For example, λ is a hyperparameter controlling how much large
coefficients are penalized. Other examples of hyperparameters are parameters
the optimizer uses, such as the learning rate, parameters indicating the depth
of trees, the number of weak learners in an ensemble method, and the sizes and
number of layers in neural networks. These parameters need to be optimized for
each problem, but they cannot be optimized using the gradients, so a grid/line

When fitting the linear model with or without ridge regression, the standardized

numerical issues if this was not done.
4.7
Decision tree – Tree
Now that the linear model and ridge regression, the focus will move on to another
type of model called a decision tree. It should be noted that, unlike a linear
model, which could predict multiple outputs using the same model, the standard
==PAGE:78==

Figure 4.4: The mean of the unsigned errors, denoted as ME, on the training

decision tree is more distinct. Each tree can only predict a single attribute1. In
truth, a linear model just parallelized this by stacking multiple models in the
matrix C. However, a decision tree cannot do this, so the labels yt will be 1d
points, e.g. the lateral irregularities of the left rail in the D1 wavelength domain.
Understanding decision trees firstly require focusing on the two words making it
up, so first, the focus is on what a tree is. A tree is based on the mathematical
concept of a graph, which is a collection of nodes and edges, where the edges
connect nodes. A classic example of a graph would be a road (or train) network,
the intersections/train stations are nodes, while the edges are the roads/tracks
connecting two nodes, and an entity travelling on the graph can move along
an edge from one node to another. A decision tree constructs a binary tree,
an example of which can be seen in Fig. 4.5. A binary tree has a single root
node, and each node has 0, 1 or 2 child nodes, meaning 0, 1 or 2 nodes under
it. A node with zero children (zero nodes under it) is called a leaf. A binary
tree’s height, or depth, is the maximum number of edges from the root to any
node, corresponding to the maximum number of edges from the root to any leaf.
When the tree is drawn as in the figure, the height is the number of layers with
nodes. All the nodes that are not leaves are called internal nodes.
Now, it is necessary to understand what a decision is, and it is relatively straight-
1A decision tree can be modified to predict tuples, but this will not be pursued here.
Outputting tuples can be seen as performing weight sharing between several trees

forward; it is a question about the features in the data. For instance, a decision
could be of the form: Is attribute 5 less than 2?, and depending on the answer,
one goes to the left or right child of the node. This process is repeated until a
leaf is found. See Fig. 4.6 for a depiction of this.
Knowing how a decision tree works also requires knowing how it makes predic-
tions and is constructed. Given a tree, some samples in the training data end
up in each leaf; say, samples 1, 2, 5 and 8 are the ones that end in the i’th leaf.
Then the prediction in the i’th leaf is the mean of the samples in the node, i.e.
predictionleaf i = y1 + y2 + y5 + y8

(4.13)

Figure 4.7: The mean of the unsigned errors, denoted as ME, on the training
and validation data when fitting the decision tree. The y-axis is
the ME, while the x-axis is the depth of the tree.
The construction of a decision tree starts with a single node, the root, labelled as
a leaf. Then a series of decisions are proposed for each leaf, and the best decision
(in terms of the loss) is added by making the leaf into a decision node and adding
two new leaf nodes beneath it. Then the process of suggesting splits is repeated
on the new leaves until either a) no split improves the loss meaningfully, b) an
initially specified depth is reached, or c) the number of samples in a node gets
under a threshold. When fitting a tree, the loss is called the impurity measure,
but the MSE is still the measure that is used.
If trees become very deep, for instance, if the maximum depth is very high, the
tree may overfit, as seen before. To prevent overfitting, the depth of the tree is
limited, and a fitted tree can be pruned – meaning that decisions are removed
after constructing the tree. For this project, only the maximum depth of the
tree will be determined using the validation ME. A plot of the ME as a function
of the depth can be seen below in Fig. 4.7. The figure shows that the optimal
depth is 7.
With knowledge of decision trees, the final classic model utilized in this project
can now be presented. However, the concepts of an ensemble method and boot-
strapping are first required.

4.8
Ensemble methods
The models presented so far are generally quite simple and therefore relatively
weak in predictive power but also easy to fit, which is beneficial for an ensemble
method. The idea behind ensemble methods is to combine the predictions of
many weak learners into a single, much better prediction. For example, when
using a random forest, 100s or 1000s of decision trees are fitted to subsamples
of the data (subsamples will be explained in the next section). The predictions
from the trees are then aggregated together using various strategies, such as a
(weighted) mean for prediction and a majority vote for classification.
Before moving on, an example might be helpful. Imagine country A and country
B, where country A is governed by a single individual, while a group or an
ensemble governs country B. The leader of country A makes all decisions on
their own. In contrast, the group leading country B makes decisions together, for
instance, by majority vote when dealing with a discrete choice (classification) or
by taking a mean aggregate when dealing with a continuous choice (regression).
A visualization of ensemble methods is shown in the figure above with 100
learners, Fig. 4.8.
4.9
Bootstrapping
The second concept required is bootstrapping, a resampling method often used
in statistics but also for training ensemble methods. In this case, each of the
weak learners is fitted to a bootstrapped subsample of the data.
Bootstrapping has been visualized in Fig. 4.9 below, but the method simply
samples the original data with replacement taking K samples B times.
I.e.
the fourth sample may occur several times in a bootstrapped sample. Usually,

Classic machine learning models
but not necessarily, the bootstrapped samples are the same size as the original
sample, corresponding to K = n. However, this is not always done, and this
is, for instance, the case for random forests presented in the next section, Sec.

4.10
Random forest – RF
The last of the classic methods presented is the random forest, which uses the
concept of an ensemble. For the random forest, the training data is bootstrapped
K times, and each bootstrapped sample is used to fit a decision tree. Hence,
the number of trees in the forest is K. See Fig. 4.10 below for a depiction of
this.
Furthermore, the size of the bootstrapped samples is set to be 100· F
K % the size
of the training set, with the factor F ∈[0, K] being a user-controlled hyperpa-
rameter. The effect is that by fixing F, the bootstrapped samples get smaller
as the number of decision trees in the random forest, K, increases.
There are then two hyperparameters to optimize, K and F. Doing a small grid
search on these yields the ME values seen in Tab. 4.2.

4.11
Results
After reviewing some classic machine learning models, this section evaluates
how well the models perform on the data using the mean unsigned error, ME,
and the quantile and maximum of the unsigned errors.
It should be noted that adaboosted trees [HSM18] and gradient boosted trees
[Fri01] were also tested. Full tables with results for the models mentioned above,
along with adaboosted trees and gradient boosted trees, can be seen in Appendix
C, including a feature importance evaluation for adaboosted trees. However,
these models did not improve the performance of the point-to-point models, so
they were omitted from the main results.
The results will include how well the models perform on the data based on the
unsigned errors, a feature importance evaluation for the random forest model,
and a prediction example for a segment of dynamics/geometry in the validation
data.
4.11.1
Errors on data
In the tables below, the errors made by the models have been summarized. The
summary is done by computing the unsigned errors and then computing the
three relevant statistics, the mean, the 95 % quantile, and the maximum. It is
done for each attribute and as an aggregate across all attributes.
The results indicate some important points regarding the problem at hand.
The longitudinal irregularities (LivLong), the lateral irregularities, are, for both
training and validation, always higher than the corresponding alignment irreg-
ularities (Allin), the vertical irregularities; this is the case across all models and
frequency bands. It is a well-known phenomenon that vertical irregularities are
easier to predict than lateral irregularities. Which is caused by the addition of
the non-linear rail-wheel forces in the lateral direction, cf. Chap. 2 on vehi-
cle modelling. Furthermore, it is generally considered sufficient to use a linear
model for the vertical irregularities [Tru22]. Then this sets an additional base-
line for future models; to beat the best case for the linear model on validation
data, i.e. the 0.33 mm ME on AllinDxD1 using the linear model with ridge
regression. Additionally, there are the benchmarks of 0.87 mm and 2.94 mm
for, respectively, the 95 % quantile and the maximum of the unsigned errors.
Hence, a model will be satisfactory if it beats all three of these for the aggregate
numbers. Using the linear model with ridge regression on lateral irregularities
was the additional benchmark for CNNs stated in Sec. 2.9 on "Target precision
of the models".
==PAGE:85==
4.11 Results
71
Most of the rows in the validation table have a low variance when looking across
the models. As a random forest cannot give a meaningful improvement over a
constant model, it suggests that any model cannot give a good result when
making point-to-point predictions from dynamics to geometry. Considering the
dynamics a railway vehicle exhibits, this is not surprising, as the vehicle may
oscillate on stretches with no irregularities because of earlier excitement of the
system [PAD19]. The dynamics at time t are not independent of the dynamics
and irregularities at earlier time points, i.e. only giving a model dynamics from
a single point in time is quite unlikely to give good results.
Thus, there is a need for models using both historic and future data. One way
to do this would be to divide the data into samples, where each sample contains
m meters of data before and after the midpoint. However, this method makes a
lot of duplicated data. It would be better to make the segments even longer but
then output more per sample. As will be seen in the next section, this naturally
leads to using a convolutional neural network.
An in-depth model error analysis is not done until the next section, with the
better convolutional neural network models. Nonetheless, it will be investigated
which features are the most important. As the random forest was the best model
in terms of the aggregated ME on the validation data, this model will be used
for this.

4.11.2
Feature importance
A random forest was fitted for each output attribute. This implies that 12 forests
were fitted. The feature importance can be determined as the Gini importance
for each attribute in each tree.
The Gini importance of feature i measures
how much the loss is improved by decisions on feature i, [Scia]. The feature
importance for a single attribute is calculated as a weighted average between
the fitted random forests, using the mean unsigned errors on the validation data
for the respective models as weights.
A figure depicting the feature importance of the input features can be seen be-
low. It is noticeable that attributes 11, 18 and 22, which are the attributes
AccB3Z, AccB5Y, and AccB6Y, all have low feature importance. Nevertheless,
as noted in Sec. 3, these sensors seemed faulty, as the data looked noticeably
different from those collected with similar data sensors. It is, therefore, rea-
sonable that these do not have much impact on the predictions. Similarly, the
model shows the velocity (attribute 1) and the curvature (attribute 38) to be
the most important.
Also, it is interesting to note that the importance of the remaining dynamics
attributes does not vary much for the random forest model. I.e. one feature is
not a lot more important than another.

==PAGE:91==
Chapter 5
Convolutional neural
networks
The previous chapter introduced machine learning and presented some classic
models. However, the previous results showed that the classic models perform
unsatisfactorily when making point-to-point predictions, which was expected
from Chap. 2 as the dynamics (and irregularities) are not independent in time.
Because of this time dependency, a point-to-point model is unlikely to yield
good results.
The following chapter will focus on using convolutional neural networks, CNNs,
to predict the track irregularities. Convolutional neural networks are one of the
key methods applied in this project. Thus, this entails a detailed description
of what they are, the fitting process and prediction results, a detailed error
analysis, and recommendations on how the model can be further improved.
Firstly, a standard artificial neural network and a motivating example for CNNs
from image analysis are presented.
This presentation demonstrates CNNs’
strengths in solving the prediction task. The motivating example is taken from
image analysis, as these examples can be easier to visualize.
The presentation of the material in this chapter draws largely on the book
[GBC16] and the lectures [Cev21c, Cev21d, Cev21e, Cev21h, Cev21i, Cev21j,
Cev21b] unless the text specifies otherwise.
==PAGE:92==
78
Convolutional neural networks
5.1
The architecture of artificial neural networks
– and why to use them
It is helpful to understand the simpler model of a standard neural network, also
called an artificial neural network, ANN, before getting into the details of a
CNN. Therefore, the architecture of artificial neural networks will be presented
first, with the motivation for using them over classic machine learning methods.
The following will assume the input is a normal row vector, similar to the input
for the classic models with xt. However, suppose the input was a matrix (e.g.
representing a greyscale image) or, more generally, a tensor (such as a colour
image or video of greyscale or colour images). In that case, the input could be
reshaped into a vector without loss of information. As for the classic models,
multiple inputs are stacked into a matrix, where each row is an input.
Neural networks work by recursively applying an affine transformation, i.e. a
linear model, followed by an activation function, which is a non-linear transfor-
mation that works element-wise. The activation function after the final affine
transformation may be omitted, depending on the task. Each transformation is
called a layer; the last layer is called the output layer, while the middle layers
are called the hidden layers. The layers can be written out in equations as seen
below, where there are k hidden layers:
1st hidden layer:
h1 = σ1 (W1x0 + b1)
(5.1)
2nd hidden layer:
h2 = σ2 (W2h1 + b2)
(5.2)
3rd hidden layer:
h3 = σ3 (W3h2 + b3)
(5.3)
...
k’th hidden layer:
hk = σk (Wkhk−1 + bk)
(5.4)
Output layer: xoutput = σoutput (Woutputhk + boutput) ,
(5.5)
here the Ws are weight matrices, and the bs are bias vectors.
The size of
a layer is equal to the length of the resulting vector, e.g.
the length of h1
for the first hidden layer in eq. (5.1). If the input is of size nin, the hidden
layers are of sizes ni, i ∈{1, 2, ..., k}, and the output is of size nout then it
follows that W1 ∈Rn1×nin, Wi ∈Rni×ni−1; i ∈{2, ..., k}, Woutput ∈Rnout×nk,
bi ∈Rni; i ∈{1, ..., k}, and boutput ∈Rnout. The layers in an ANN are also called
fully connected layers, as the weight matrices are dense. As such, every element
in layer i is influenced by every element in layer i −1. I.e. every element in a
layer is connected to every element in the previous and next layers.
The σis are activation functions, which must be non-linear, as if σi is a lin-
==PAGE:93==
5.2 Motivation for convolutional neural networks
79
ear function, then the i’th layer could be omitted. This result is because two
affine transformations joined by a linear function correspond to a single affine
transformation. The transformation σ for the output is optional and is used
depending on the task. For instance, when doing classification, it is common to
use the softmax function, eq. (5.6), on the output. Sec. 5.7 will go over relevant
activation functions and the differences and advantages of these.
Softmax(xi) =
exp(xi)
P
j exp(xj)
(5.6)
An ANN is a simple yet powerful tool. The main results from [Cyb89, Theo-
rem 1] and [HSW89] imply that the class of ANNs with a single hidden layer is
a universal function approximator [Cev21h]. So an ANN can approximate any
function arbitrarily well, given that the hidden layers are sufficiently large.
When the output and potentially the input are one-dimensional vectors, then
the Stone-Weierstrass and Fourier theorems [Sto48, Wik22b, Wik22a] say that
polynomials and Fourier series also are universal function approximators. Sim-
ilar results apply for wavelets and other methods, so why are neural networks
so popular? That is because the number of parameters grows exponentially in
the size of the input/output when using these other methods. In contrast, the
parameters of ANNs grow polynomially [Rac22b]. The slower growth makes
neural networks a tractable method for approximating a function, thus making
them very popular.
5.2
Motivation for convolutional neural networks
Despite the previously mentioned advantages of ANNs, there are still downsides.
Although the number of required parameters grows much slower than for other
methods, there are still cases when ANNs are too big and too inflexible. The
former refers to the fact that an ANN often has too many parameters to optimize
for modern hardware despite the abovementioned benefit. Alternatively, the
optimization method or available data are insufficient to optimize the number
of parameters. The latter refers to the fact that ANNs are restricted to input
and output of a fixed size. A more flexible network which uses the data more
efficiently is therefore needed.
Consider the task of classifying whether an image is of a ring or a straight line
and that the location of the symbol may vary in the image, as seen in Fig.
5.1. It would then be beneficial to detect whether the image simply contains
a straight line. The line should be located regardless of where in the image it
==PAGE:94==
80
Convolutional neural networks
is. A stencil could be made, which checks each potential location. However, an
ANN would require a copy of this stencil for every potential location. These
copies imply that the network needs many parameters, all representing the same
feature detector.
Image 1 - line
Image 2 - ring
Image 3 - line
Image 4 - ring
Figure 5.1: Four images for the example image classification task with labels.
The symbol (ring or line) may change location in the image, which
is the source of the difficulty. The frame for each image indicates
the boundary of the image and is not part of the values in the
image, so there are no straight lines in an image containing a ring.
This problem leads to the unique component in CNNs, the convolution operator.
The operator works by taking a kernel and then convolving the input with the
kernel. If the kernel matches the above-mentioned stencil, then the convolution
results in the same output as the ANN. Thus, this single operator removes the
need for many copies of the same stencil.
In a technical sense, the convolutional operator corresponds to weight sharing
between parameters in an ANN. Besides a drastic reduction in the number of
parameters, the operator also benefits from being agnostic to the input size. For
example, if the former task of classifying the symbol instead involved locating the
symbol in the image, then a network consisting entirely of convolution operators
would allow to output an image of the same size as the input, where the predicted
location is highlighted, regardless of the size of the input.
CNNs are more than 25 years old, but the source of the considerable popularity
in the past ten years is primarily the publication of AlexNet [KSH12] for classi-
fying images, which beat the previous state-of-the-art methods by a significant
margin.
==PAGE:95==
5.3 The architecture of convolutional neural networks
81
5.3
The architecture of convolutional neural net-
works
With knowledge of why to use CNNs, it is necessary to know how to use them.
These details will entail the terminology used to describe CNNs, ways to regu-
larize them, and more.
CNNs are sometimes used for classification; an example was the case with
AlexNet [KSH12], where fully connected layers, the layers from ANNs, are used
in the end. However, this section only describes the components used by the
networks in this project to simplify matters. This description will not discuss
pooling operations or adding fully connected layers.
The description of CNNs will primarily follow the very detailed and thorough
description found in [GBC16] with elements from [Cev21h].
Like the ANN, the CNN consists of several layers, each applying the convo-
lution operation and then followed by an activation function, i.e.
the affine
transformations are replaced by a convolution. This project works with multi-
ple time-series, or a time-series with multiple channels, as inputs and outputs.
A sample is therefore structured as (channels, time)1.
This structure is not
much different to how the data was kept for the classic models. However, the
difference is that neighbouring rows are also neighbours in time, and thus the
rows are no longer independent of each other.
If a convolutional layer is of size c and uses a kernel size of k, then it has c
kernels, and for each kernel, it outputs a time-series. A kernel is applied along
the time-series and works on all channels simultaneously. Furthermore, each
kernel may add a bias. The kernel also has a stride parameter, which controls
how much the kernel is moved. If the stride is 2, then the kernel is moved two
samples each time. This parameter is efficient for downsizing and is often used
in, e.g. a max pooling operation. As downsizing is not desired, the stride will be
mostly fixed at 1. However, when using the interpolated data, one layer should
have a stride of 3.
A visualization of a convolutional layer with c = 3 kernels, k = 3 kernel size and
a stride of 2 is shown in Fig. 5.2.
As the kernel needs to overlap with the input, there is a loss in the time direction.
However, some layers may use padding where the input is extended, such as by
adding constants (e.g. zeros) or reflecting the input. The padding avoids the loss
1Note that the data is stored in a matrix, but the channels are the columns, not the rows.
==PAGE:96==

Convolutional neural networks

Figure 5.2: Example of the convolution operation in a convolutional layer.
The input is convolved with three kernels; the result is the three
coloured time-series. The kernel is moved two samples in the time
direction each step, corresponding to a stride of 2.
in length, but artefacts may occur at the edges. Therefore, padding is limited
and only used in a few cases to avoid these artefacts.
5.4
Fitting neural networks – backpropagation,
batch learning, and optimizers
This section presents how the parameters in a CNN are fitted to data.
Fitting the classic models was relatively straightforward.
The constant and
linear models (with or without ridge regression) had closed-form solutions, and
the decision trees could be relatively quickly constructed. However, the CNNs
are complex, and because of this, there is no closed-form solution. Thus, an
iterative learning method must be applied. The following parts will therefore
present what iterative learning is and how it is done for neural networks, NN.
Iterative learning means that the model coefficients, such as weights and bi-
ases, are updated in steps from an initial guess. A classic algorithm for this is
Newton’s method, which uses the gradient, Jf, and hessian, Hf, to update the
weights w of some function f, which is being minimized. The method uses an
==PAGE:97==
5.4 Fitting neural networks – backpropagation, batch learning, and
optimizers
83
initial guess w1, and then the weights are updated using the step,
wi+1 = wi −γHf(wi)−1Jf(wi),
(5.7)
where γ ∈[0, 1] is called the step size or learning rate.
These updates are
repeated until one deems that the method has converged. The process might be
halted after a fixed number of steps or when the change ∥wi+1 −wi∥is small.
Newton’s method is effective, as it converges very quickly in terms of the number
of steps taken. However, when training a neural network, one might have thou-
sands, millions, or even billions of coefficients, and then it becomes infeasible
to calculate the Hessian. Both because it takes too much memory and also be-
cause it would take too long. Methods using the Hessian are called second-order
methods, and neural networks are fitted using first-order methods, i.e. methods
which only use the gradient. Thus, avoiding the expensive calculation of the
Hessian.
The arguably simplest first-order method is gradient descent (GD), where the
step is given by
wi+1 = wi −γJf(wi).
(5.8)
Many other first-order methods exist, and some will be mentioned momentarily,
but it is first relevant to touch on how the gradients are computed.
Due to the layered structure of a neural network, the i’th layer can be seen as
σi(Axi−1 + b) where σi is applied element-wise and xi−1 is the output from the
previous layer (the convolution can also be seen as an affine transformation).
Because of this structure, the chain rule gives an efficient method to compute
the gradient.
This method is called backpropagation [RHW86, GBC16] and
this method, to efficiently compute the gradient of the loss function w.r.t. the
model coefficients, is a fundamental reason for the success of neural networks
and thus deep learning. See [GBC16, Chapter 6.5] for a detailed description of
the backpropagation method. This project treats backpropagation as a black
box that returns the gradients of the networks.
How the gradients are utilized to update the parameters is more influential
regarding fitting the networks.
There are many optimizers, i.e.
algorithms
for how to update the model coefficients, and to complicate matters further,
combining optimizers [Rac22a] may be relevant. The following paragraphs will
discuss relevant optimizers and advantages and disadvantages with these, but
first, the data need to be discussed one more time.
The gradients of f w.r.t. the model coefficients are estimated using the data.
However, it is typical to use a mini-batch, which means that not all the training
==PAGE:98==
84
Convolutional neural networks
data is used to estimate the gradient. Using mini-batches has two effects, the
gradient has a higher variance, but more importantly, it has the effect that the
gradient can be estimated much faster. Experience has shown that having more
updates of the weights is better than having more accurate gradient estimates.
The objective function, the loss, is often non-convex, and the noisier gradients
can also help the algorithm converge to a better solution. The gradients on a
batch of the data will be denoted as ∇f(wi; B) where B is the batch of data.
If one was working on an image analysis task, each sample might be an image;
here, a segment is a continuous segment of track data. The batch size is then
the number of images or segments of rail irregularities in a batch, i.e. with a
batch size of five, the model receives five distinct images or track segments, and
the gradient is calculated from the loss on the batch. The training data consists
of multiple batches, and a pass through all the batches is called an epoch.
When the geometry data is stored as a batch, it is a 3d tensor with dimensions
(batch, channel/feature, time).
All optimizers have one or more hyperparameters. The learning rate is always
present for any optimizer.
As mentioned earlier, when presenting Newton’s
method, the learning rate controls the step size or how much the weights are
changed. This hyperparameter will be tuned as the first because it is one of the
most important hyperparameters when training a model. Tuning the learning
rate will be presented as part of the results, along with tuning the other parts
of the model. The remaining hyperparameters specific to each optimizer will
almost always use the default settings. If changes are made, it will be clear
when discussing the optimizer.
One of the first optimizers tested is stochastic gradient descent (SGD) which is
almost the same as gradient descent. However, the gradient is calculated on a
mini-batch rather than the entire training data. The update is,
wi+1 = wi −γ∇f(wi; B), i ≥1.
(5.9)
SGD often includes a momentum term which adds an exponential average of all
the previous gradients. Let g0 be a zero vector; the update is then given by,
gi = µgi−1 + γ∇f(wi; B),
(5.10)
wi+1 = wi −gi, i ≥1,
(5.11)
where µ is the momentum factor set to 0.98 in this project. Adding momentum
to the algorithm improves the convergence rate significantly, and experience
has shown that this method gives the best convergence when using an optimal
learning rate and momentum factor. However, it can be quite sensitive to the
hyperparameters, and the benefits may not even be there [SMDH13].
==PAGE:99==
5.4 Fitting neural networks – backpropagation, batch learning, and
optimizers
85
One of the most widely used optimizers is Adam [Kin15], as it usually gives
good convergence while being quite forgiving in selecting an optimal learning
rate. For SGD, the learning rate significantly influences whether the algorithm
can converge to an acceptable solution, but Adam can almost always converge to
a decent solution. This benefit is because Adam uses the previously calculated
gradients to tune the learning rate for each parameter. However, as mentioned
above, SGD with momentum can sometimes be preferred after a suitable model
has been found, as it can outperform Adam when the learning rate is tuned
correctly.
Experience has shown that Adam is an excellent initial choice when dealing
with a physics-informed neural network, PINN [Rac22b, RPK17]. However, it
is a good idea to switch to a method such as BFGS or L-BFGS when Adam is
starting to converge [Rac22a]. These methods will be referred to as PolyOpt
from the algorithm doing this in Julia. Due to the optimization objective used
here, PolyOpt is unlikely to give meaningful benefits for the CNNs used in this
project. However, this will be discussed later in this chapter and Sec. 7.2 on
future work.
The learning rate is a crucial hyperparameter, but it can be beneficial to use a
high value initially and then decrease it. This method is referred to as learning
rate scheduling, and a learning rate scheduler simply alters the learning rate
during training based on some given rules. There exist many choices for this,
and two examples are the fixed step learning rate scheduler which decreases the
learning by a factor β ∈[0, 1] every k’th epoch, where β and k are user-defined
hyperparameters. Another simple method is the multistep schedule. Here the
learning rate is again decreased by a constant factor β, but the user defines the
epochs at which it is done. An example of the former is scaling the learning
rate by β = 0.8 every 50 epochs. While an example of the latter is to scale the
learning rate by β = 0.9 after epochs 40, 70, 120 and 150.
There are much more complicated schedulers, but these are not tested; hence,
these will not be discussed. Initial tests do not indicate that more complicated
schedulers would improve the model.
Now that the model has been defined and the optimization has been described,
the focus will move to regularization.
==PAGE:100==
86
Convolutional neural networks
5.5
Regularization
When fitting the linear model in the previous chapter to the artificial data,
the model was shown to overfit if it was too "free". Unfortunately, a neural
network can easily overfit, as the networks are often quite large. It is, therefore,
necessary to explain how the model, or network, can be regularized.
5.5.1
Early stopping
Early stopping is likely the simplest regularization method available. Usually,
when fitting a model, one often stops training after a certain number of epochs
with no regard for the change in the model coefficients during each step. Thus,
the number of epochs is chosen as a relatively high value to ensure the model
does not stop prematurely.
When the model coefficients are fitted using the iterative method, it is common
to see that both the training and the generalization loss initially decrease. How-
ever, after a certain point, the model starts overfitting; hence, the training loss
decreases, but the generalization loss increases, as depicted in Fig. 5.3. So to
attain the best possible model w.r.t. the generalization loss, the training should
be halted just as the generalization loss plateaus. For this purpose, the loss of
the validation set is periodically calculated during training, saving the model
with the best/lowest loss.
Training iteration
Loss
Training loss
Generalization loss
Minimum generalization loss
Figure 5.3: Example of how the model starts overfitting after some epochs.

5.5 Regularization

5.5.2
Dropout
Dropout is another widely popular technique, improving performance without
increasing the computational need.
In addition, it has been shown to im-
prove the generalization loss when using a limited amount of data [SHK+14,
EHSK+14, WFGCB14].
The idea of dropout is to omit varying parts of one’s model during a training
batch. A helpful view is to see the model as a collection of feature detectors to
understand the method. For example, a linear model has one feature detector
for each input value. Dropout then omits a selection of the feature detectors, for
instance, by random selection, during a training batch. So each time the model
is given a batch of training data, the network is sampled accordingly. Fig. 5.4
shows a visualization of dropout during training, where each node is a feature
detector.
Given k feature detectors, the random selection can be made in two ways.
Firstly, setting a number k′ < k one selects k′ feature detectors to remove,
i.e. a uniform selection of the
  k
k′

sets of feature detectors. Secondly and more
commonly, by setting a percentage p, the i’th feature detector is omitted with
probability p – hence on average, p · k feature detectors are omitted. Recall in
==PAGE:102==

Convolutional neural networks

a CNN that xi is a tensor of shape (batch, channel, time), and each channel
corresponds to a feature detector. A channel is omitted by setting all values to
zero. Fig. 5.5 shows a visualization of dropout using a probability p = 1/3.
When dropout is utilized, it is added at the end of a convolutional layer, and
the dropout value p (chance each channel is dropped) can be layer-specific.
It is now necessary to distinguish between the two modes a network can be in, the
training and evaluation mode. The training mode is used when the networks’
weights are updated, and dropout works as described above.
However, the
evaluation mode applies when the network is used in the real-world, i.e. used
with validation or test data, and then dropout does not remove feature detectors.
However, if the i’th layer used a dropout percentage of pi, then during training
a feature detector in the i’th layer is present with probability 1 −pi. However,
the feature detector is always present during evaluation, and its weights are
therefore scaled by the factor 1 −pi. The scaling is done to ensure the expected
output of a feature detector is the same during training and evaluation.
A way to view why dropout is an efficient method is that it decreases co-
adaptations between feature detectors. Co-adaptation means that one feature
detector relies on the output from another feature detector. However, since a
feature detector may regularly be dropped, the other detectors cannot rely too
much on its output.
Another view is that dropout is an efficient way to train an ensemble of models,
usually done by training separate models. However, training a single network
is computationally expensive, so fitting an ensemble is infeasible [EHSK+14].
So instead, dropout mimics the ensemble method seen previously. Here, the
learners are each possible network after dropping features, and two learners
may share many model coefficients.
==PAGE:103==
5.5 Regularization
89
5.5.3
Weight decay – l2-norm regularization for iterative
methods
The use of ridge regression benefitted the linear model greatly, as seen in the
example using artificial data in Sec. 4.6. Neural networks can also benefit from
adding the norm of the weights to the objective function. When fitting using
an iterative method, it should be noted that using the l2-norm, with scaling λ,
the model coefficients are not just updated with the gradients. However, 2λwi
is added to the gradient of coefficient wi. So when using SGD, this corresponds
to multiplying the coefficients by 1 −2λ, then updating using the gradient as
expressed below [Vas19].
wi+1 = (1 −2λ)wi −γ∇f(wi; B).
(5.12)
Therefore, the l2-norm regularization is referred to as weight decay when fitting
neural networks. However, this is misleading when using other optimizers like
Adam, as the scaling by 1 −2λ does not occur [LH17]. However, this is not
a crucial detail, and the method will still be referred to as weight decay, even
when using the Adam optimizer.
5.5.4
Batch normalization
The last regularization technique that will be discussed is batch normalization
[IS15], which works similarly to how the data was standardized as mentioned
in Sec. 3.8. As marked earlier in Sec. 4.4, the data needed standardization
to alleviate numerical issues when fitting a linear model. Batch normalization
serves the same purpose but with some additions. Also, batch normalization
can be used between layers, and the focus is first on why that is an advantage.
The output of one layer affects the subsequent layers.
Hence, updating the
weights in the i’th layer affects the input to subsequent layers and, thereby, the
gradients. Because of this, the learning rate needs to be low. Batch normaliza-
tion somewhat alleviates this when applied between layers, so the learning rate
can usually be increased while getting the same precision. The higher learning
rate makes the network converge faster, reducing training time.
Let x be a
single feature, then recall that standardization, S(x), performed the operation,
S(x) =
x −E(x)
p
V(x) + ϵ
,
(5.13)
where E(x) is the expected value, or mean, of x, V(x) is the variance of x,
and ϵ = 10−5 is added for numerical stability. Batch normalization, B(x), does
==PAGE:104==
90
Convolutional neural networks
mostly the same, but it adds two parameters γ, and β for each channel which
are learned from data,
B(x) =
x −E(x)
p
V(x) + ϵ
γ + β.
(5.14)
For batch normalization, the mean and variance are estimated from the batches
using an exponential average with a factor µ = 0.1. So if ˆx is currently estimated
statistic and ˆxnew is the statistic estimated from the current batch of data,
then the update ˆx = (1 −µ)ˆx + µˆxnew is done. Statistics are computed for
each channel; hence, the statistics are taken across both the batch and time
dimensions.
5.6
How to use the validation data – Cross-val-
idation
The requirement of a validation set lowers the data available for training and
thus increases the expected generalization loss. Since the validation loss can be
seen as a random variable with a mean at the generalization loss, the validation
set should be as large as possible to improve the generalization loss estimate.
However, this removes data from the training set.
One way to circumvent this issue is to do cross-validation. In cross-validation,
CV, the training set is usually split into K disjoint sets, and the same model
is fitted K times. The i’th time the model instance is fitted using the i’th set
as the validation set, and the remaining K −1 sets are joined together to form
the training set. Afterwards, the generalization loss is estimated as the average
validation loss across the K losses.
As noted earlier in Chap. 3 the training dataset for CNNs was segmented, as
seen below in Fig. 5.6a, giving six disjoint segments for the validation data.
Cross-validation is then done by training six networks, and when training the
i’th network, the i’th validation sample is used as the validation data. Finally,
the remaining validation samples are added to the training data.
Regarding the earlier discussion on batch learning, the batch size is one, and
each track segment is the longest possible continuous segment. In Fig. 5.6b
this is visualized for i = 2. Thus, each green segment in the figure is a training
sample.

5.7
Activation functions
This section is then dedicated to introducing standard activation functions.
==PAGE:106==
92
Convolutional neural networks
The activation function is important as it introduces the required non-linearity
to the network. There are several common choices, such as the rectified linear
unit, called ReLU, and variations of it, such as Leaky-ReLU. The activation
function should be fast to compute and have gradients that behave nicely; i.e.
the gradients should neither explode nor vanish. The ReLU activation function
satisfies this and is widely used, but work has shown that other functions may
be better choices. Additionally, if one wanted to fit a physics-informed neural
network, to be discussed later in Sec. 7.2, ReLU might not be the best choice
[Rac22b]. The following functions will be tested;
ReLU(x) =
(

, x > 0
0
, otherwise
(5.15)
ELU(x) =
(

α exp{(x −1)}
, otherwise
(5.16)

(

, otherwise
(5.17)

(

(5.18)

(
x

αcx
, otherwise ,
(5.19)
all of which are applied element-wise. The αs in ELU and Leaky-ReLU are
tunable hyperparameters. However, the default values of 1 and 0.01 will be
used for simplicity. In RReLU, the α is a random variable which is drawn from
the uniform distribution U(0.125, 1/3) at each evaluation [XWCL15]. Lastly,
the PReLU works as the Leaky-ReLU, but the αc is a parameter learned from
data, and the c indicates that each channel has its own learnable α.
For a detailed analysis of why these perform better than ReLU, the reader is
referred to [CUH16]. The short version is that the mean gradient is closer to
zero, as non-negative outputs are allowed, which provide stability when close to
a minimum. A figure can be seen below in Fig. 5.7 of the activations functions
tested in this project.

5.9
Hyperparameters to be tuned
This paragraph will give an overview of the relevant hyperparameters and other
choices that need to be optimized. The following section presents results using
CNNs, and tuning of the hyperparameters.
The first set of hyperparameters are for the model; the number of layers, size of
the layers, length of kernels, dropout percentages, and any potential parameters
for the activation functions.
Then there are hyperparameters such as the learning rate and other optimizer-
specific parameters, parameters for any potential learning rate scheduler, and
the amount of weight decay.
Additionally, there are "hyperparameters" such as the choice of the optimizer(s),
non-linear activation function(s), and learning rate scheduler. However, only the
step learning rate schedulers are tested, where the learning rate is multiplied by
a constant factor after a certain number of epochs. This limitation is done to
simplify the fitting process, as mentioned earlier.
There are also choices such as whether to use batch normalization and, if so,
where. However, this mainly impacts the learning rate by allowing a higher
learning rate such that the model may converge faster. So for the overall fitting
process, this is not very important, as the interest is getting a good fit more
than the time to fit a model.
The tuning process needs to start with some CNN models. Two initial networks
are used, see Tab. 5.1 for the architectures, and it is chosen based on previous
work in [Ple21]. The two slightly different architectures were tested initially to
understand how their differences would affect the optimal learning rate. The
models are fitted to the interpolated data presented in Sec. 3.9, and because of
==PAGE:109==
5.9 Hyperparameters to be tuned
95
this, the models must have one layer with stride three and all other layers with
stride one. Initially, the training utilizes the Adam optimizer and all models have
batch normalization in the beginning, since this is not altered at any point, the
overviews of models will not include it.
The tuning process was done in the following order:
1. Tune the initial learning rate
2. Tune the architecture (number of layers, size of the layers, length of ker-
nels)
3. Tune the learning rate again
4. Add dropout
5. Add batch normalization after all hidden layers
6. Test multiple seeds to test the effect of the initialization and dropout
sampling
7. Tune the kernel lengths
8. Tune activation function
9. Add weight decay
10. Test optimizers (plots not available due to server issues at the time of

5.10
Results – tuning the model
This part will present results from fitting CNNs to the data and tuning the
models. Some proposed changes did not significantly impact the fits, so some
of the above tuning steps will be moved to Appendix D. Specifically, the second
tuning of the learning rate, batch normalization, and the test of multiple seeds
will be moved to Appendix D. In some cases, numerous settings were tested,
making the plots crowded and difficult to view.
Therefore, the plots in the
results only include relevant settings, and the appendix will include additional
plots with all settings.
The intermediate results will only include the aggregated ME for the models,
which is done to make visualizations and tables easier to interpret. The next
section will follow up with a detailed analysis of the final model. A model is
initialized by sampling the model coefficients from various distributions, and the
samples depend on the seed. For reproducibility, the seed is fixed at 10, and the
test of multiple seeds shows that this is not an issue.
5.10.1
Tuning the learning rate
The first hyperparameter to test is the learning rate, and from prior experience,
the expected range wherein the optimal value can be found is around 10−5
to 10−2.
Furthermore, experience has shown that a range of values can be
optimal, and the lowest optimal learning rate is around half the largest optimal
learning rate. Hence, an optimal value should be found by testing log-spaced
values, where the i’th value is less than two times the previous value. These
considerations give 11 log-spaced values.
Plots of the learning curves can be seen in Fig. 5.9 below. Furthermore, the
minimum validation ME is given for each learning rate and each model in Tab.
5.2.
The best combination is Model 1 with a learning rate of 3.16−4. However, the
models generalize poorly to new data, as the validation ME is much higher than
the training ME. While the training does not seem to have finished after the 300
epochs, as the training ME is still decreasing noticeably, the validation ME has
plateaued, making further training unlikely to be beneficial. Compared to the
benchmarks of 0.1 mm and 0.33 mm, the models are still far off for the validation
data, but they improve over the best classic model, which had a validation ME
of 0.83 mm. Further improvements are therefore needed, and the next area is
the architecture. It is also notable that the same learning rate is optimal for
==PAGE:111==
5.10 Results – tuning the model

both models. It should therefore not be of great concern to tune the learning
rate later on.

Table 5.2: Minimum validation ME for the tested learning rates for the two
initial models. The minimum value has been highlighted in bold.
which the stride is set to three is also moved based on hand-tuning. The location
of stride three had a small but not insignificant effect on the ME. However, the
training runtime is much higher when the stride is in the second rather than the
first hidden layer. This increase in runtime holds even if the model is relatively
small. Therefore, the runtime can be made much faster by making this change.
Nonetheless, the models do not perform well enough to justify this choice.
In this round, many models were tested (15 different architectures), most of
which were omitted to make the plots readable. Appendix D contains learning
curves for all tested models. Because of the many models, a shorter format
for the table with the architectures is needed. As all models use ReLU after
every hidden layer and nothing after the output, this need not be specified. The
format will then only specify the CNN operators’ parameters, as explained in
the caption for Tab. 5.3 showing the 15 models.
The minimum validation MEs are shown in Tab. 5.4 for the 15 models, while
Fig. 5.10 shows the learning curves for selecting the best models. From this,
the best is Model 7, using slightly larger layers, i.e. more channels. However,
the improvement is not that large, so rather than testing even larger or deeper
models, the focus will be to improve Model 7 through other means, such as
learning rate and dropout.

5.10.3
Dropout
The second learning rate tuning showed that the learning rate need not be
modified, so now the focus will move on to regularization of the model. One
very efficient and effective regularization technique is dropout, which is the first
technique to be tested, as it likely can significantly improve the models. Dropout
is applied at the end of each hidden layer and possibly on the input. The dropout
is controlled by the layer-specific parameters pl for l ∈{0, ..., 4} where p0 is the
dropout applied to the input, and pi, i ∈{1, ..., 4} is the dropout applied at the
end of the i’th hidden layer.
In total, 20 combinations of dropout percentages are tested, as seen in Tab. 5.5.
Due to the large number of combinations, the plots of the learning curves will
omit some combinations, but the full plot is included in Appendix D. The learn-
ing curves can be seen in Fig. 5.11, and the minimum validation MEs can be
seen in Tab. 5.6. From this it is noticeable that only combination 19 improves
the model, and the improvement is slight. Looking at the training ME, the
issue seems to be that the model has difficulties converging. This observation
can be seen from the values being much higher than earlier, and interestingly
combinations like 1, 5, 7, and 11 start overfitting very early in the training pro-
cess. These results are contrary to what was expected. However, due to time
constraints, it is not investigated further.
It should be noted that due to the high volatility, a learning rate scheduler was
employed for combinations 19 and 20. The learning rate is thus scaled by 0.4
after the 300th and 400th epochs. This schedule is kept for subsequent results.
5.10.4
Tune the kernel lengths
Because the addition of regularization has so far not improved the results much,
the model is further tuned. The kernel lengths earlier were chosen such that at
least one layer had a kernel of 200 m. This length implies that the convolutional
operator in that layer would be able to capture long wavelengths, which is crucial
as information about long wavelengths is needed for the D3 wavelength domain.
However, the kernel length might benefit from further tuning, so this is done
here. Nine models are then proposed based on Model 7 as seen in Tab. 5.7.
Some models only have three hidden layers; in these cases, the last dropout, p4,
is ignored (dropout was already removed from the fourth layer as p4 = 0).
The learning curves are shown in Fig. 5.12 and the minimum validation ME are
==PAGE:117==
5.10 Results – tuning the model

reported in Tab. 5.8. It is immediately visible that Model 24 improves upon the
previously best model. Furthermore, from the learning curves, it can be seen
that it is still beneficial to have the learning rate scheduler, as the validation
ME is quite volatile until the learning rate scheduler decreases the learning
rate. Additionally, the training MEs are much lower than the validation MEs,
so the model is slightly overfitting. The focus will first be on testing activation
functions and then on regularizing using weight decay.

5.10.5
Choice of activation function
As mentioned earlier, ReLU might not be the best activation function to use,
so here the focus will be on testing the four other activation functions presented
earlier.
The activation functions are applied after each CNN operator in a
==PAGE:121==

hidden layer, just as with ReLU. The results of the tests are shown in Fig. 5.13
and Tab. 5.9. These show that ELU is the best activation function, beating the
others by a wide margin. Subsequent tests will therefore swap ReLU for ELU.
More tuning is needed, as the models still do not reach the benchmarks. It is
interesting to note that RReLU cannot converge; it seems that the stochastic
behaviour is undesirable for this problem.

This regularization is done here through weight decay or regularizing the l2-

in Fig. 5.14, and the minimum validation ME is shown in Tab. 5.10. These
show that using weight decay with λ = 10−3 improves the model slightly, and
the satisfactory ME level is almost achieved.

5.10 Results – tuning the model

5.10.7
Choice of optimizer
The previous results are very close to the satisfactory level for the ME, so
the last test is to change the optimizer and see if this can improve the results
slightly, thereby getting to the 0.33 mm level. When substituting the optimizer,
it is necessary to tune the learning rate, which is done by hand-tuning each
optimizer.
The learning curves are unavailable at the time of writing due to technical issues
accessing DTU’s HPC cluster after a security upgrade following an attempted
hack of DTU’s system.
5.10.8
Final model
The last parts have focused on tuning the model; thus, this part is dedicated
to summarizing the final model. Which makes it possible to contrast the final
model against the initial model, and more importantly, it makes it clear what
the final model is.
The final model is fitted for more epochs to ensure it has converged. Addi-
tionally, another round of tuning the dropout percentages was performed; the
results are included in Appendix D. The second round was done as the previous
dropout results were contrary to what was expected. Firstly, dropout mainly
had a negative impact on the validation ME, and the dropout percentages that
worked were surprisingly low and did not improve the model much. However,
this second round of tuning gave more expected results, as the dropout rates
were higher at around the expected 0.5.
The optimization is done using the Adam optimizer with an initial learning
rate of 3.16·10−3, and a learning rate scheduler is used. When training for 800
epochs, the learning rate scheduler decreases the learning rate by a factor of five
after the 700th epoch. The validation ME is still decreasing after 800 epochs,
so training should not have been halted at this point.
However, due to the
previously mentioned technical issues accessing DTU’s HPC cluster, it was not
possible to fit the model any more before the deadline.
The model uses the ELU activation function, dropout, weight decay (with λ =
10−3), and batch normalization. A visualization of the model can be seen in
Fig. 5.15.
The learning curves have been plotted below in Fig. 5.16. The figure shows

5.10.9
Compute time required
If the CNN should be used to make real-time predictions, then it must be able
to make predictions for more than 400 km of track per hour. Otherwise, the
model would fall behind very high-speed railway vehicles when in operation.
Training one instance of the final model takes around 73 minutes on a high-end
GPU (an NVIDIA A100 GPU), which must be done six times. However, the
six instances can be trained in parallel. Additionally, the model needs only be
fitted once, so the 73 minutes is not a concern.
It takes 60.72 seconds to evaluate the final model on the test data, which is
35.30 km long, using a personal desktop with a low to mid-range GPU (a GTX
970 GPU). Hence, the model can evaluate 0.58 km of track data per second
(=2092.64 km of track data per hour), well above the speed of railway vehicles.
The model can, therefore, easily be used for real-time processing of the dynamics.

5.11
Error analysis
The previous section fine-tuned a CNN, and this section is focused on investi-
gating the errors made by the model in detail to see whether these hint at areas
for potential model improvement. The errors of the greatest interest are the
validation errors, but time is also spent on the training errors.
The error analysis will first focus on the aggregated errors through various statis-
tics and then followed by relevant plots of the errors. This broad analysis is done
to identify where the significant errors are in the data. Afterwards, a more de-
tailed analysis is done at the identified locations. This part aims at deducing
the cause for these errors and, thereby, what can be done to improve the model
most effectively.
==PAGE:126==
112
Convolutional neural networks
This analysis will be followed by a brief discussion on how the model could be
improved based on observations from this chapter. A more in-depth discussion
of future work will be done later in Sec. 7.2. Transfer learning was also tested
as part of this project to overcome one of the potential problems of data insuf-
ficiencies. However, these results have been moved to Appendix E as the use of
transfer learning did not improve the model. The optimization got trapped in
a local minima and failed to converge.
The error analysis will be done in two parts. Firstly, looking at all errors and
summarizing these through key statistics and plots.
Secondly, based on the
overviews, it is then of interest to localize the significant errors.
For these
purposes, it is relevant to remember the three benchmarks for CNNs, reiterated
below.
(iv) 0.33 mm for the mean of the unsigned errors,
(v) 0.87 mm for the 95 % quantile of the unsigned errors,
(vi) 2.94 mm for the maximum of the unsigned errors.
Six model instances are fitted when the model is used, one for each validation
region.
When validation errors are stated, the errors for the i’th validation
region use the instance fitted with the i’th region as the validation set.
5.11.1
Key statistics and visual overview
The previous results only showed the validation ME aggregated across the vali-
dation area and rail irregularity feature. The table below, Tab. 5.11, summarize
the aggregated statistics of interest for the validation and training errors.

==PAGE:130==

Convolutional neural networks
Before moving on to the detailed part of the error analysis, two more figures
will be presented with scatter plots of the errors against the true values for the
training and validation data. First, collecting all the errors for the training and
validation, the figure below, Fig. 5.19, is made. A few noteworthy things can
be seen from this.
Firstly, the dots seem to be tracing lines in the graphs. These traces indicate that
the errors are correlated in time, i.e. along the track. However, this correlation
is not surprising. The predictions are correlated due to the CNN operator, and
the irregularities, or targets, are also correlated in time. Therefore, it stands
to reason that the errors would also be correlated in time, especially since, as
discussed below, the model underpredicts.
Another noteworthy thing is that the errors span a narrower interval than the
true values; thus, most predictions get the correct sign. This conclusion can
be gathered as if a prediction has the incorrect sign, then the error is at least
the true value. However, since the errors generally are smaller in magnitude
than the true values, this cannot be the case. In relation to this, it can be
seen that for large irregularities, where the true value is larger than 7.5 mm
in magnitude, then no points lie in the first or third quadrant. I.e. when the
irregularities exceed ±7.5 mm, the model always underpredicts.
Thirdly, the points seem to have a negative correlation. The correlations can
be found to be -0.43 for the training and -0.41 for validation. Hence, a positive
true value usually indicates a negative error and vice versa. Since the error is
the prediction minus the true value, then the correlations imply that the model
generally underestimates the magnitude.
Lastly, in connection to the last point, it is relevant to note that some points in
the validation plot deviate from the negative correlation. These are also much
larger than the other errors. However, considering the previously seen maximum
unsigned errors, a reasonable assumption is that these points belong to the fifth
validation area.
In Fig. 5.20, the validation plot has been redone with the fifth validation re-
moved. This figure shows that the assumption was correct, as the points which
deviated are no longer there. Moreover, the negative correlation is much clearer,
reflected in the correlation coefficient now being -0.47 rather than -0.41.
The above analysis shows that the fifth validation region contains some partic-
ular issues in the data, which need to be investigated further. In addition, the
last two plots showed that the model generally underpredicts the magnitude, as
the true values are larger than the errors, and there is a negative correlation
between the errors and the true values.

There seem to be data issues in certain areas. However, before visualizing the
problematic regions in detail, it can be good to get an impression of a more

the magnitude of the irregularities; however, it can generally capture the true

is shown in Fig. 5.22. The figure shows that the model has difficulties around
positions 108000-108200 m. The 108000-108200 m is plotted again in Fig. 5.23
for another irregularity feature. There are still issues at the same part of the
tracks. This agreement across features indicates that the issue is in the input.
