The input is plotted in the area around positions 108000-108200 m. However,
there are 38 input features, so to simplify, a plot with eight features is shown
in Fig.
5.25.
This figure shows that the vertical acceleration of axel box 2
(AccB2Z) has an abnormality after position 108050 m. The sensor data are
zeros, which was done as part of the data engineering because the sensor had
outliers at these positions. However, this seems to have impacted the model
negatively.
The statement above is merely a hypothesis; however, this hypothesis can be
tested. Almost all the irregularity features had very high maximum errors in
the fifth validation area. If it is the faulty sensor causing these large errors,
then all the large errors should lie close to the faulty sensor data.
Close in
this context means faulty data is used to make the predictions. Looking into
the data, the faulty region is specifically from position 108052.5 m to position
108058.5 m. The model uses 202 m of data before and after a point to make
a prediction, so all the large errors should be between position 107850.5 m and
position 108260.5 m. Fig. 5.26 shows all the errors for the 12 geometry features
in the fifth validation area. The figure shows that all the large errors are in the
red region (the affected region). Hence, the plots support the hypothesis that
the faulty data cause the large errors.
Furthermore, the figure also shows that the errors are generally within the sat-
isfactory level for the remaining parts. However, the level is exceeded a few
times, but the errors in these cases are barely above the threshold, and inspec-
==PAGE:135==
5.11 Error analysis
121
tions showed no apparent signs of faulty data.
In Fig. 5.17, it was seen that there were a few other areas where the satis-
factory level was exceeded. Looking into these, the sixth validation area is of
particular interest, as this is the only area where the threshold is noticeably
exceeded. However, the threshold is only meaningfully exceeded for the vertical
D2 irregularities for the right rail around position 139470 m, plots included in
Appendix D.
The input is then investigated in this area, and a curious observation is made.
In the area surrounding the significant error, the vertical accelerations of the
bogies are irregular.
In Fig.
5.24, the lateral and vertical accelerations for
axel boxes 1 and 2 and the corresponding accelerations of the front bogie are
plotted.
These were not chosen for any particular reason, as all eight bogie
measurements do the same. At the time of writing, these irregular accelerations
cannot be explained, as neither the wheel sets nor the car body exhibits any
abnormal behaviour in this region. However, they are likely the cause for the
large error at this track segment.
It should be noted that a passing railway vehicle can significantly impact the
dynamics, as can other local changes such as changes in the ground/soil, driving
on a bridge, and driving in a tunnel. The model has no knowledge regarding
such external impacts, and this can therefore cause significant prediction errors.
However, these external factors should affect more than just the vertical acceler-
ations of the bogies, so it is unlikely to be these factors which cause the observed
behaviour. Another plausible explanation is that it is caused by measurement
errors coupled with artefacts from signal processing done before the analogue
to digital conversion occurs.

==PAGE:139==
5.12 Focus areas to improve the model
125
5.12
Focus areas to improve the model
When doing some hand-tuning, the model seemed to achieve much better fits
to the data if the validation set was kept small.
This observation indicates
that either the estimate of the generalization loss is very volatile, or the model
may lack data. The latter can more generally be seen as the model lacking
information rather than just data.
Earlier in Chap.
2 a great deal of time
was spent extending a railway vehicle model to curved tracks, which introduced
knowledge of the forward problem that has not been used. The forward problem,
going from rail irregularities to dynamics, can help in the fitting process by using
physics-informed neural networks, PINNs. In these, the physics are included in
the objective function and thus used during the fitting process. The idea is that
the output is used as input to the forward problem, i.e. the system of ODEs.
The estimated accelerations using the predicted irregularities are then compared
to the actual accelerations.
Following the error analysis, a second iteration of data modifications, where
faulty samples are removed rather than zeroed, is required. The regions where
this was done had a significant negative impact on the results. This change
could potentially improve the model significantly, as it might not be forced to
underpredict as much. Another option is to use another loss function, such as
the l1-norm, which is not as sensitive to outliers. The errors could get up to 15
mm in the areas with faulty data; thus, the squared error is 225. Meanwhile,
most other errors are less than the ME of 0.32 mm, which imply squared errors
around 0.1. Hence, the faulty region’s errors have a 2250 times larger impact
on the loss than most other data points. This larger impact heavily skews the
optimizer towards improving the loss for the faulty data rather than all other
points.
5.13
Evaluating on the test data
Since no further model selection or tuning will be made, the model can be
evaluated on the test data. The model is applied to the test data by aggregating
the predictions for the six models using the mean. The six models are the six
instances of the model that is fitted using different validation areas. The key
statistics for the unsigned errors can be seen in Tab. 5.12. As can be seen,
the model satisfies the mean level, but not the 95 % quantile nor the maximum.
Moreover, this is without considering the stricter goals for (partially) solving the
problem. Thus, improvements are still needed, but the task is likely solvable.
In addition, it is noteworthy that the errors in the D1 wavelength domain are
==PAGE:140==
126
Convolutional neural networks
noticeably lower than for D2 or D3, and the model almost passes the partially
solving criteria when restricting to these. However, the amplitudes are generally
higher for D3 than D1, and the absolute errors would, all else being equal, be
higher for D3.
Based on this, the mean errors are scaled by the maximums for each feature,
and the result can be seen in Tab. 5.13. The table shows that the predictions
for D3 are, in fact, relatively better than the predictions for either D1 or D2.
As mentioned earlier, [HYY+22] sets a benchmark of 0.25 mm and 0.45 mm of
the ME in the wavebands [3 m, 42 m] and [42 m, 120 m] using deep learning.
These are the best results found in the literature review, and the presented
model is comparable to or even slightly better than these. The mean unsigned
errors for the final model in D1 ([3 m, 25 m]), D2 ([25 m, 70 m]) and D3 ([70 m,
200 m]) are 0.21 mm, 0.25 mm and 0.49 mm. The errors in D1 and D2 are thus,
on average, 0.23 mm and hence slightly smaller than the errors [HYY+22] have
in [3 m, 42 m], so the model can be said to outperform on the short wavelengths.
Similarly, the average errors in D2 and D3 are 0.37 mm, which is smaller than
the errors [HYY+22] have in [42 m, 120 m]. So the final model is also better on
the longer wavelengths. The wavelength overlaps are not perfect between here
and in [HYY+22], but this comparison should be as good as possible.
The primary issue in the comparison is the difference in data. It is very much
possible that the final model here would perform much worse when applied to
the data from [HYY+22], or similarly that their model would be much better
when applied to the data used here. However, without a standard set of data,
this cannot be known for sure.

Chapter 6
Conformal prediction
The previous chapters have shown how to make predictions for rail irregularities.
However, when using a model in the real-world, it is just as important to have the
uncertainty of a prediction as it is to have the prediction itself. The predictions
will contain some errors, and when using a prediction interval, the goal is that
the true value is contained in the interval at least (1 −α) · 100 % of the time.
This interval is called an α level prediction interval.
Touching one last time on the example with Kepler’s laws, these also require
uncertainty intervals.
Recall that the example problem in Chap.
4 was to
estimate β in T 2 = βa3 such that the orbital period can be estimated from the
orbital radius. Several factors, such as an incorrectly estimated β, may influence
the predictions. The goal is then to estimate a time window for what the orbital
period likely is.
Conformal prediction [VGS05, BHV14, SV08] is a powerful data-driven tool
which can provide such prediction intervals for any machine learning model.
What makes conformal prediction so interesting is that several of the methods
provide virtually assumption-free guarantees for the interval; i.e. no data or
model assumptions are required [Mapb].
The conformal prediction methods aim to solve the following problem: Given a
training set of n data samples which can be used to fit a model m(·), for the
==PAGE:144==
130
Conformal prediction
unseen sample, xn+1 with true value yn+1, give an α level prediction interval, C.
I.e. give an interval which satisfy P(yn+1 ∈C(xn+1)) ≥1 −α. Some methods
only give a 2α level prediction interval, but they still typically contain yn+1
100 · (1 −α) % of the time when applied to data [BCRT21].
In a basic sense, the methods from conformal prediction can be seen as using
the errors from the training or validation set to estimate the α level prediction
interval. The following will first describe how conformal prediction is done using
a selection of the methods described in [BCRT21] and [Mapb].
6.1
Methods for conformal prediction
This section will present the conformal prediction methods based on [BCRT21],
and [Mapb]. As the jackknife, jackknife+, and jackknife-minmax methods are
computationally heavy, this overview will omit a description of these, but the
curious reader is referred to [Mapb] for details.
6.1.1
Naïve
The naïve method fits a model, m(·), using all n samples as the training set;
let bm denote the fitted model. The method then computes the unsigned errors
on the training data, giving the set {| bm(xi) −yi|}i∈{1,...,n}. The interval for the
new data sample is then
bCnaïve
α
(xn+1) = [ bm(xn+1) −bq+
α ({| bm(xi) −yi|}i∈{1,...,n}),
bm(xn+1) + bq+
α ({| bm(xi) −yi|}i∈{1,...,n})].
(6.1)
bq+
α ({| bm(xi) −yi|}) is the 1 −α quantile of the unsigned errors.
Similarly,
bq−
α ({| bm(xi)−yi|}) will be used later to denote the α quantile of the set {| bm(xi)−
yi|}i∈{1,...,n} as used in [BCRT21]. This method performs poorly, as the model
may overfit, which makes the estimated intervals too narrow.
6.1.2
Holdout
The holdout method splits the n samples into two sets, Strain and Sholdout. The
model is then fitted on the data in Strain giving bmholdout
train
while the samples from
==PAGE:145==
6.1 Methods for conformal prediction
131
Sholdout is used to estimate the 1 −α quantile of the unsigned errors; i.e.
bCholdout
α
(xn+1) = [ bmholdout
train
(xn+1) −bq+
α ({| bmholdout
train

bmholdout
train
(xn+1) + bq+
α ({| bmholdout
train
(xi) −yi|}i∈Sholdout)].
(6.2)
This method is insufficient if the amount of data is small compared to the
required amount of data to fit the model. The issue in this case is that either
|Strain| is too small or |Sholdout| is too small. In the former case, the model fits
the data poorly, making predictions bad, and in the latter case, there is a high
variance on the estimated quantile, making the intervals poor.
This problem is the same as the issue with estimating the generalization loss
using a validation set in the same manner. Furthermore, the problem can be
solved similarly using cross-validation, CV. There are three methods using this,
CV, CV+ and CV-minmax.
6.1.3
CV
The first CV method is similar to how cross-validation is handled for estimating
the generalization error. The data is split into K disjoint sets, S1, S2, S3, ..., SK,
of equal size. Then the k’th set is used as the validation set, while the remaining
K −1 sets are joined as the training set; let bmCV
k
denote the model when fitted
using this training data. Then for each k ∈{1, ..., K} evaluate the errors when
predicting on the validation set and join all these; i.e. the set seen below,
R =

6.1.4
CV+

[Mapa, Mapb].

6.2
Results for the best convolutional neural net-
work
The conformal predictions will be demonstrated using the CV methods with
the six instances of Model 24, each fitted to one of the six validation areas.
In addition, the new data will be taken from the test regions to demonstrate
how well the intervals perform on unseen data. Neither model coefficients nor
hyperparameters have been tuned to this data.
Firstly, it is necessary to determine whether the standard CV method can be
used. Recall that the CV method should only be used if the estimators are
stable. A segment of irregularities with predictions for the six models can be
seen below in Fig. 6.1. The figure shows that the predictions vary across models,
but the mean fits quite well. The models are, therefore, not stable estimators,
so the CV method should not be used.
The options are then the CV+ and CV-minmax methods. The latter generally
==PAGE:148==
134
Conformal prediction
produces wider intervals but has better theoretical guarantees.
In Fig.
6.2,
the α = 5% prediction intervals for the above example have been shown. It
can be seen that the CV-minmax intervals are slightly wider than the CV+
intervals. Furthermore, the intervals mainly capture the true values even when
the aggregated model deviates from the true values. However, the intervals are
imperfect, and some values are not captured.
Tab. 6.2 gives some statistics for the two methods. Both methods capture more
than 95 % of the true values, even though the α level is set at 5 %. The intervals
are typically relatively narrow, <2.33 mm, so if the irregularities are large, then
the prediction intervals will likely capture them.
The models can therefore
produce usable prediction intervals, as they are not too wide. Nevertheless, the
intervals can become relatively wide, as seen for the maximum width at 4.54
mm and 5.25 mm. By improving the model further, these interval widths will
also decrease.
True value within CV+ intervals
95.76 % of the time
True value within CV-minmax intervals
97.18 % of the time
Average width of CV+ intervals
1.78 mm
Average width of CV-minmax intervals
2.33
Maximum width of CV+ intervals
4.54 mm
Maximum width of CV-minmax intervals
5.25

CV-minmax methods. First, the fraction of true values lying inside
the interval. Then the average width of all intervals predicted by a
method. Lastly, the maximum width of the intervals predicted by
a model.

6.3
Compute time required
Adding these methods to the prediction pipeline also requires that the prediction
intervals can be constructed in real-time; hence, they must process more than
400 km of track per hour when given the predictions. Otherwise, they would
fall behind very high-speed railway vehicles.
The CV-minmax prediction intervals are much faster to compute than the CV+
intervals. The former takes around 0.12 seconds, while the latter takes around
12 minutes to compute the intervals for the entire test data. As the test data
contain 35.30 km of track data, this implies that the CV-minmax method can
compute intervals for 294.17 km of track per second (=1,054,217.07 km of track
per hour), while the CV+ method only computes 0.05 km of track per second
(=176.5 km of track per hour). However, this is using a personal desktop and
without optimizing the code. Using better hardware and optimizing the code,
the CV+ processing could likely process the data in real-time. These runtimes
disregard the time needed to make the predictions, but this depends on the
model. Recall that the final CNN could process over 2,000 km of track per
hour, and thus the CV+ method would be a bottleneck, while the CV-minmax
would not decrease the throughput.
==PAGE:151==
Chapter 7
Conclusion and Future
Work
7.1
Conclusion
In this project, a vehicle model of the Cooperrider’s bogie was extended to allow
for curved railway tracks, cf. Chap. 2. The model was then implemented in
Julia, based on an earlier C++ implementation from previous work, which did
not handle curved tracks. Real-world data from an ETR500 passenger train was
then analyzed and used for fitting data-driven machine learning methods, most
notably convolutional neural networks. Finally, conformal prediction was used
to assess the uncertainty of the predictions.
The vehicle model extension allows the generation of artificial dynamics for
curved tracks. Nevertheless, using these did not prove to be beneficial for solving
the inverse problem.
Data issues were identified and addressed during the data processing phase
using standard practices. However, modifying rather than discarding those track
segments ended up hurting the final model, Sec. 3.3 and Sec. 5.11.
The main prediction problem was then attempted in Chap.
4 using classic
machine learning method, making point-to-point predictions. These achieved a
==PAGE:152==
138
Conclusion and Future Work
mean unsigned error of 0.83 mm, which is well above the benchmark of 0.1 mm,
Sec. 4.11, and as such, the methods did not solve the problem, nor were they
below the satisfactory 0.33 mm.
The convolutional neural networks were then fitted in Chap. 5, and these gave
a satisfying result of 0.31 mm on the test set; however, the problem is still
not solved, Sec. 5.13. In addition, the 95 % quantile and maximum unsigned
errors for the final model were 0.98 mm and 7.61 mm, which are higher than the
values for a satisfying model of 0.87 mm and 2.94 mm. The model performed
much better on lateral irregularities than on vertical irregularities, which is
contrary to expectations, as vertical irregularities are usually easier to predict
as the dynamics are linear.
Nevertheless, the results for short and medium
wavelengths (D1 and D2) are satisfactory regarding the mean unsigned error.
The overall results are comparable to or better than the best solution found in
the literature. The final convolutional neural network can evaluate more than
2000 km of track data per hour. Thus, the model can be applied for real-time
processing, Sec. 5.10.9.
Lastly, prediction intervals were estimated for the test set, capturing 97.18 % of
all true values using intervals with an average width of 2.33 mm and a maximum
width of 5.25 mm, Sec. 6.2. Hence, the intervals cover most true values; however,
the width is a bit higher than what ideally would be the case. These intervals
use the CV-minmax conformal prediction method. It can evaluate more than
1,000,000 km of track data per hour, meaning the method can be applied to
in-service vehicles for real-time processing of the prediction data, Sec. 6.3.
==PAGE:153==
7.2 Future Work
139
7.2
Future Work
This section will look at possible future work based on the work and results from
this project. The work can broadly be divided into four areas: fixing known data
issues, vehicle modelling, data generation, and condition monitoring.
The first thing to work on is fixing the data issues identified in the project’s
final stages. This task would imply removing the faulty sensors as suggested by
Stefano Bruni, cf. footnote in Sec. 3.3. Furthermore, the outliers around 108
km should be handled differently, as discussed in Sec. 5.11.
Possible additional work in vehicle modelling is due mainly to the code being
translated to Julia and the scientific computing methods this enables. However,
the model could reasonably be extended to a complete wagon model, as men-
tioned in Sec. 2.3. The first option enabled by Julia is to study the forward
sensitivities of the railway vehicle. The forward sensitivities are the derivatives
of the state variables w.r.t. the parameters over time. An example of a forward
sensitivity is the derivative of the front wheel set’s lateral position w.r.t. the
mass of the wheel sets. The goal is to know which parameters significantly af-
fect the dynamics and thereby affect the predictions. This idea is more loosely
related to this project.
As seen in Appendix E, transfer learning did not provide beneficial results.
Although, several reasons could cause the negative results. For instance, there
could be a mistake in the data formatting, or the ODE system may not represent
the dynamics well enough. Exploring the latter allows for some interesting work
in scientific machine learning. Namely, to use a data-driven approach to fix
the ODE system. Currently, the assumption is that an ODE can describe the
vehicle dynamics
du
dt = f(u; t),
(7.1)
u is the state at time t, and f is the true function mapping the state and
time to the derivative. The vehicle model aims at approximating f with the
function ˆf. However, the model fails if the approximation deviates too much
from the true function f. The goal would then be to approximate the difference
h(u; t) = f(u; t) −ˆf(u; t) using data-driven machine learning methods.
For
instance, an artificial neural network, NN(u; t). The ODE would then become,
du
dt = ˆf(u; t) + NN(u; t).
(7.2)
This type is in the literature known as Neural Ordinary Differential Equations
[CRBD18]. Note that the ˆf(u; t) is not even necessary, as given sufficient data
==PAGE:154==
140
Conclusion and Future Work
and a big enough neural network, the problem could still be solved. However,
adding known dynamics is beneficial and will only help the training process.
Fitting a neural network to an ODE is also numerically challenging, so including
any known dynamics will help.
The third area of future work relates to the data part of the machine learning
methods. This area of work should not have any influence on the model type,
but the goal is similar to the purpose of Chap. 3 on data engineering. The ideas
can be grouped into either modifying real-world data or generating artificial
data. The data can be modified by removing sensors or augmenting data.
The first modification implies that some sensors are removed from the data. For
example, it could be that only the velocity, curvature and car body data sources
are available. In addition, the author in [KYT02] discusses that the axle box
and bogie sensors are more exposed to the elements and, therefore, likely to fail.
The axle box sensors can also be disturbed by significant temperature differences
from heat generated by the braking, as discussed in [WRYS15]. Based on this, a
relevant question could be: Can the rail irregularities be sufficiently accurately
predicted from sensors installed inside the car body?
Another possible modification is to add noise to the input data to mimic mea-
surement noise. This data modification was suggested in [WRYS15]. Concerning
this, it is also necessary to examine the sensors’ accuracy. A training process
could then sample from a relevant noise distribution each time a sample is used.
I.e. new noise samples are added to the training dynamics every epoch.
The following techniques generally require more data than the techniques used
in this project; however, this can somewhat be alleviated by the suggested data
generation. CNNs are a relatively simple type of sequence-to-sequence neural
networks; however, there are other options which may generalize better. Two
such neural networks are residual neural networks, ResNets, commonly used in
conjunction with Neural Ordinary Differential Equations, and recurrent neural
networks, RNNs. Future work could focus on using one of these techniques or
employing other deep learning methods such as transformers [VSP+17].
In unsupervised learning, there exists a group of models called autoencoders.
These perform a (lossy) data compression, but the reconstructions are generally
excellent. Fitting an autoencoder to the irregularities and then predicting these
compressed irregularities could be another good course of action. This strategy
has been used in many state-of-the-art sequence or image processing models.
In relation to the Neural Ordinary Differential Equations, there is a type of
neural networks called physics-informed neural networks, PINNs, [RPK17] as
discussed in Chap. 5. The idea is to augment the objective function with a term
==PAGE:155==
7.2 Future Work
141
that describes how well the neural network satisfies the physics. The method has
shown to be very efficient when only relatively few data samples are available.
It would be incorporated in this project using H to calculate the dynamics, the
accelerations, based on the predictions of the rail irregularities. PINNs would
particularly benefit from using a PolyOpt technique. The strategy of starting
with Adam and then switching to BFGS was developed for fitting PINNs in Julia
[Rac22a]. The benefits are partially due to Adam’s convergence issue. However,
another way to improve this would be to use AcceleGrad/UniXGrad, which have
better convergence guarantees than Adam [Cev21j, LYC18, KLBC19].
The last two suggestions for future work would be to combine classic signal
processing methods with the neural networks or update previous predictions.
In the case of the former, classic techniques are also used to process or make
predictions, and these processed signals or predictions are concatenated with
the dynamics to give the input used by the neural networks [MCC+19, Edi21].
The latter refers to a similar approach where earlier predictions are added to
the input. The idea here is that the system gives day-by-day predictions, but
rather than discarding previous predictions, the system can use the predictions
from the previous day to update the irregularities.

Conclusion and Future Work

Appendix C
Classic models extra results

the models are interchangeable in terms of performance on both the training
and validation data, the Adaboost model outperforms on the training data.
However, this is likely overfitting, but the validation errors are, despite this
overfitting, equivalent to the other ensemble methods.

C.2
Feature importance
The two models both show the velocity (attribute 1) and the curvature (at-
tribute 38) to be the most important. But while the features have much higher
importance for the adaboost than for the random forest.
A very significant difference in the feature importances between the two models
is that the random forest uses the accelerations (the dynamics) much more for
its predictions than the adaboost model does. Also, it is interesting to note that
the importance of the various dynamics attributes does not vary much for the
random forest.

D.1.2
Learning rate check
As the learning rate is significant, and the architecture was modified, this section
is dedicated to testing the learning rate again to ensure the value is still optimal.
For this purpose, three learning rates below and three above the previously found
are chosen. The tested learning rates are, {5 · 10−5, 1 · 10−4, 2 · 10−4, 6 · 10−5, 1 ·
10−3, 2·10−3}, and the learning curves have been plotted in Fig. D.2 while Tab.
D.1 shows the minimum validation ME. From this, it is seen that the learning
rate 2·10−4 is slightly better than the previously used 3.16·10−4. However, the
model converges slower due to the lower learning rate. As mentioned earlier, the
models were improving very slightly for Fig. 5.10. Model 7 had 100 additional
epochs here, and the difference may be because of this. The learning rate is
therefore kept at 3.16 · 10−4 for the next tests.

batch normalization between every layer. This is because the learning rate might
then be increased. After adding batch normalization, the following four learning
rates are tested {8.53 · 10−4, 2.30 · 10−3, 6.22 · 10−3, 1.68 · 10−2}. The learning
curves have been plotted in Fig. D.4. The figure shows that the validation ME
is worse than before, so it is not beneficial to increase the learning rate even
with the addition of batch normalization.

D.1.5
Test multiple seeds to test the effect of the initial-
ization
The models now contain additional randomness from the addition of dropout.
Therefore, it is relevant to consider how significant this impact is. The coef-
ficients are randomly initialized, and dropout relies on random samples. This
randomness can all be somewhat controlled by the seed, and several seeds will
be tested, and the variance examined to check the effect of initialization. Before,
the seed was fixed to 10, but here seeds one to eight will be tested, and Fig. D.5
shows the learning curves while Tab. D.2 shows the minimum validation ME. As
can quickly be noticed from the table, the variance is relatively small, but some
minor differences can be seen during the fitting. Nonetheless, it is therefore not
necessary to test multiple seeds while fitting a model, as the variance is small.

D.1.6
Dropout round 2
The first round of dropout gave some weird results, as the dropout percentages
were low, and the benefit was small. Therefore, a second round was done and
results will be shown here for combination 23 to 26. The numbering continued
from round 1, and two combinations were dropped. The test is done slightly
==PAGE:175==
D.1 Extra results
161
different here than before, as some hand-tuning showed that the dropout per-
centages could be much higher on the first and second hidden layers. The models
will not be fully fitted to save some time, so the test will focus on three things,
1) should there be dropout on the input, 2) should there be dropout on the
last hidden layer, and 3) is the first and second hidden layers sensitive to the
dropout percentages. The combinations in Tab. D.3 are chosen based on this.
As can be immediately seen from Fig. D.6 there are two groups in the end,
combinations 23, 24, 25, and 28, and combinations 26 and 27. The difference
between the two groups is that combinations 26 and 27 are the only to apply
dropout on the input. In the other group, combination 23 is slightly better than
24 and 25, and they all beat 28. So having a much higher amount of dropout on
the first and second hidden layers than earlier is beneficial, and dropout on the
third layer may hurt the performance. So based on these results the dropout is
set to 60 % on the first and second hidden layers, and none is applied elsewhere.

Appendix E
Transfer learning
This chapter will very briefly test transfer learning for the problem at hand.
The idea of transfer learning is that a model is fitted to another set of data, and
the model is then applied to the task at hand. The benefits of transfer learning
are threefold, firstly the model should fit faster to the task at hand, secondly a
more accurate may be achieved, and thirdly fewer data samples are required.
The motivation behind transfer learning has natural examples from the real
world. Imagine a school chess captain faces a football player in a basketball
game. They might both have played the same amount of basketball, but the
football player is still likely to win. Simply because they can draw on the abilities
they gained from football when playing basketball. The same is the purpose of
transfer learning, the abilities are now feature detectors, and the idea is that
these feature detector which work well on another task will work well on the
task at hand; at least when compared to a randomly initialized model.
The issue then becomes what this related model should be, and here it will be
a CNN using the final CNN model settings and fitted to simulated data. The
simulated data is as described in Chap. 2. The irregularities outputted from the
simulation have been filtered, which had the effect of down scaling frequencies
of interest. To fix this issue, the irregularities are scaled such that the maximum
is 5 mm for all 12 geometry features.
==PAGE:180==
166
Transfer learning
In the figures below, learning curves for the final model fitted to the generated
data, Fig. E.1, and using said fitted model on the real data, Fig. E.2, can be
seen. These show that the model is unable to converge, but it is unclear what
is causing this. Potential issues could be the generated data, particularly there
could easily be a mistake when changing data formats. Due to time constraints,
this is not investigated further.

242function SHE!(p::railway_vehicle_structure)

p.creep[i,2]
,→
253
+ sqrt(p.point[i,4] * p.point[i,5]) * p.point[i,10] *
p.creep[i,3]);
,→
254
255
fnorm = sqrt(p.fx[i]^2 + p.fy[i]^2) / (nu * p.point[i,2]);
256
257
if fnorm < 3

factor = 1 - fnorm / 3. + fnorm^2 / 27.;

p.fx[i] *= factor;
260
p.fy[i] *= factor;
261
else

p.fx[i] *= 1 / fnorm;
263
p.fy[i] *= 1 / fnorm;
264
end

end

J[20, 25] = (2 * p.k3) / p.mw;
587
# FW roll circular acceleration
588
J[22, 13] = (2 * p.k3 * p.d1d1) / p.Iwx;
589
J[22, 21] = (p.data[2,1] * (p.data[2,6] + p.data[2,3]) - p.data[1,1] *
(p.data[1,6] + p.data[1,3]) - 2*p.k3*p.d1d1) / p.Iwx
,→
590
# RW roll circular acceleration
591
J[24, 13] = (2 * p.k3 * p.d1d1) / p.Iwx;
592
J[24, 23] = (p.data[4,1] * (p.data[4,6] + p.data[4,3]) - p.data[3,1] *
(p.data[3,6] + p.data[3,3]) - 2*p.k3*p.d1d1) / p.Iwx
,→
593
# bogie frame vertical acceleration
594
J[26, 17] = (2 * p.k3 ) / p.mf
595
J[26, 19] = (2 * p.k3 ) / p.mf
596
J[26, 25] = (-4 * p.k3 - 2*p.k5 ) / p.mf
597
J[26, 26] = (-2 * p.D1 ) / p.mf
598
# bogie frame pitch circular acceleration
599
J[28, 17] = (-2 * p.b * p.k3 ) / p.mf
600
J[28, 19] = (2 * p.b * p.k3 ) / p.mf
601
J[28, 27] = (-4 * p.b * p.k3 * p.b ) / p.mf
602
# Rolling constraint p.beta1 (front wheelset)
603
J[29, 3] = (-p.data[2,2] * (p.data[2,6]+p.data[2,3]) - p.data[1,2] *
(p.data[1,6]+p.data[1,3]) - 2*p.k3*p.d1d1 *u[13])/p.Iwy
,→
604
J[29, 13] = ( -2*p.k3*p.d1d1*u[3]) / p.Iwy
605
# Rolling constraint p.beta2 (rear wheelset)
606
J[30, 7] = (-p.data[4,2] * (p.data[4,6]+p.data[4,3]) - p.data[3,2] *
(p.data[3,6]+p.data[3,3]) - 2*p.k3*p.d1d1 *u[13])/p.Iwy
,→
607
J[30, 13] = ( -2*p.k3*p.d1d1*u[7]) / p.Iwy
608
609
610end
